[1] Prologue
[2] A play is like a meal...
[3] The prologue is like a pre-prayer before the meal.
[4] Act 1
[5] Morgue, February 2nd, evening 9:30 pm
[6] On an unusual February afternoon, the deep-sea trawler Ravinia D, after a long journey across the Atlantic, passed Cape Hook and sounded its piercing whistle as it approached the port of Hancock. The ship made its way down the bay, its bow cutting through the waves, its stern trailing behind, with a modest catch and a filthy deck that resembled a slaughterhouse. The stench of the Atlantic seawater was nauseating, and the crew cursed the captain, the ocean, the fish, the dark sky, and the barren coastline of Staten Island on the port side. Bottles were passed around, and the sailors shivered under their foul-smelling waterproof clothing.
[7] A large man leaning on the railing, gazing pensively at the blue waves, suddenly straightened up, his face flushed red and his eyes bulging, shouting loudly. The crew looked in the direction indicated by his index finger, about 300 feet away, where a small, black, and undoubtedly dead body was bobbing up and down in the bay.
[8] The crew was thrilled. "Hard a-port!" The helmsman leaned into the wheel, barking the order.
[9] The Ravinia D began to clumsily move to the left side of the ship, each joint creaking as it did so, like a vigilant beast stalking its prey, circling closer and closer to the object. The crew was excited and eager, tapping the water with their fishing rods, impatient to reel in the strangest catch of the day.
[10] Fifteen minutes later, the object lay sprawled on the wet deck in a pool of foul-smelling seawater, disfigured and rotting but unmistakably a man. The state of the corpse's decay suggested that the man had been submerged in the deep sea for several weeks, battered by the tides. The crew stood silently on the deck, their hands clasped behind their backs, staring at the corpse. No one touched it.
[11] Thus, with its nostrils filled with fish stench and salty wind, York Blackett began his final journey. The filthy trawler was his coffin, the rough sailors in fish-scaled rough cloth and unshaven faces were his pallbearers, and the sailors' whispered curses and the wind blowing through the narrow bay were his requiem.
[12] The wet nose of the Ravelin D gently sliced through the water, its ropes tied to a small dock on the Bettley shore. The crew brought back an unexpected cargo, the captain shouting and the harbor officials nodding in understanding, briefly inspecting the slippery deck. The small Bettley Harbor Office telephone rang loudly. York Blackett lay beneath a waterproof tarps.
[13] But this calm did not last long. An ambulance arrived, and the white-clad medical staff carried away the wet corpse. The funeral procession left the sea, the loud siren playing a requiem, and York Blackett was taken from downtown Manhattan to the city morgue for identification.
[14] His life was strange and mysterious. On December 21, last year, four days before Christmas, the elderly Emily Blackett, who lived in the northern part of Washington Square in New York City, reported her husband missing to the police. He disappeared without a trace on that morning when no one was paying attention, leaving the red brick mansion that represented the Blacket family's wealth, without saying goodbye to anyone.
[15] The old man's whereabouts were unknown, and Emily Blackett had no explanation for her husband's disappearance. The missing persons office suggested that Blacket had been kidnapped and that someone might come to collect a ransom, but this theory was soon debunked when the wealthy Blacket family did not receive any word from any supposed kidnappers. Other newspapers had various theories: one claimed that Blacket had been murdered – anything involving the Blacket family was possible. The Blacket family denied this possibility, stating that York Blackett was a quiet, unassuming man with no friends, and so far, the investigation had found no enemies. Another newspaper, perhaps based on the Blacket family's bizarre and lively history, speculated that the old man had simply run away – fleeing his domineering wife, his annoying, rebellious children, and his neurotic home. However, this theory was also rejected, as the police pointed out that his personal bank account showed no unusual activity. Also, due to this fact, the theory that a "mysterious woman was involved in the case" was debunked. Old Emily Blackett, who was angry at this suggestion, insisted that her husband was 67 years old – a man of that age was highly unlikely to leave his family and property for a small flame of passion.
[16] After five weeks of relentless investigation, the police came to a conclusion – suicide. It seems that the police were right this time.
[17] Detective Sergeant Sam, from the New York City Police Department's homicide unit, served as the priest for York Black's rough funeral. He was a competent and unattractive man: a hard, ugly face, a broken nose, and floppy ears. You would think he was a retired heavyweight boxer, with his knuckles broken from years of beating criminals, his head with gray and red hair, stern eyes, and a ruddy complexion. He gave the impression of being solid and reliable, and as a police officer, he was quite straightforward and honest; however, after years of almost hopeless struggle, he had grown old.
[18] This case was different. From the report of the disappearance, the search for the missing person, to the discovery of the mangled corpse, and the sufficient evidence for identification, everything was open and clear. However, since there was a theory of murder, the inspector believed that he had a responsibility to dispel doubts and bring the matter to a close.
[19] Dr. Thuring, the medical examiner of New York County, gestured to his assistant to move the naked corpse from the autopsy table to the trolley. Thuring's short, fat German body bent over a marble sink, washed his hands, disinfected them, and then dried them thoroughly. Once he was satisfied with the drying of his small, fat hands, he took out a toothpick with tooth marks and began to thoughtfully pick out the teeth. The inspector blew out a breath, the job was done. Once Dr. Thuring began to dig out the tooth decay, it was time for conversation.
[20] They followed the trolley carrying the corpse to the morgue's storage room, no one spoke, and York Black's corpse was dumped onto a flat surface. The assistant turned to inquire: "Push it into the wall cabinet?" Dr. Thuring shook his head.
[21] "Well, doctor?"
[22] The medical examiner removed the toothpick: "A clear case, Sam. From the lungs, it's clear that the person died almost immediately after being submerged."
[23] "So he drowned?"
[24] "No, he didn't drown. He was poisoned."
[25] Detective Sam frowned at the corpse on the slab: "So it's a murder, doctor. We were wrong about the suicide note. It might have been staged."
[26] Dr. Thuringer's small eyes behind his old-fashioned gold-rimmed glasses gleam, and a bald head with a gray woolen cap: "Sam, you really are a straight shooter. Poisoning doesn't necessarily mean murder... yes, there's cyanide in his system, which means what? I'd say the person stood on the ship's edge, swallowed cyanide, and then fell or jumped into the water. By the way, it's seawater. Is that murder? Sam, you were right the first time, it's suicide."
[27] The inspector looks skeptical: "Great! So he died around the time he came into contact with water - died from cyanide, huh? Perfect."
[28] Dr. Thuringer leans against the corpse board, looking sleepy, a man who often looks tired.
[29] "It doesn't look like a murder. There are no suspicious signs. The seawater has a preservative effect, you know? You don't know that? There are only a few bone bruises and skin abrasions, undoubtedly the result of the corpse colliding with the seabed sediment. Obvious bruises, and the fish had a feast."
[30] "Hmm, but his face is blurred, that's a fact." The victim's clothes are placed on a chair next to him, in tatters. "How did we not find him before this? The body can't just drift for five weeks, can it? Possible?"
[31] "It's simple, really childish, you blind ones!" The forensic expert picked up a torn, wet piece of clothing from the corpse and pointed to a large hole on the back of it. "Was it the fish? Pfft! This hole was made by something big and sharp. Sam, the body was caught by a submerged tree stump on the seabed and only the tide or other waves finally released it, perhaps a storm before the rain. That's why you couldn't find him for five weeks."
[32] "So, from the location where the body was found," the chief inspector pondered, "it's easy to piece together the story. He swallowed poison, jumped off, say, from the ferry in Stadeng Island, and drifted out through the narrow bay... What about the things found on the body? I'll look at them again."
[33] Sam and Dr. Shering walked over to a table where several items were placed: some rotten, torn, and unusable paper, a heather pipe, a box of wet matches, a keychain, a leather wallet soaked in seawater, with a few bills, coins of various sizes. On the other side of the table, a heavy signet ring was placed on the dead man's left ring finger, or the engagement finger, with two silver engraved initials YH. However, the chief inspector was only interested in one thing - a tobacco pouch. It was made of fish skin and had a waterproof effect, and the tobacco inside was still dry. They had already found a folded piece of paper inside that had not been damaged by seawater. The message on it was written in indelible ink, the handwriting was neat and almost perfect, like a typewriter print.
[34] The message read:
[35] 19XX, December 21st, Dear reader: I am committing suicide in a fully conscious state of mind.
[36] Yorke Hate
[37] "Simple and to the point," Dr. Shelving commented. "I'm going to commit suicide. I'm very clear-headed. It's a one-liner that summarizes a novel, Sam."
[38] "Well, spare me the tears," the sergeant grumbled impatiently. "The old lady's coming up. Notify her to identify the body." He quickly pulled a thick blanket over the corpse's legs. Dr. Shelving muttered a German phrase and stepped aside, his eyes shining.
[39] A group of people entered the morgue in silence: a woman and three men. It was not surprising that the woman was walking ahead of the three men. She seemed to be someone who was always in charge and in command. She looked old and hard, like a wooden fossil, with an eagle's beak nose, white hair, and eyes that did not blink like an eagle's. Her short, thick chin showed that she never lowered herself to anyone... This was Mrs. Emily Hate, known to two generations of newspaper readers as the "rich family" of Washington Square, the "monster", and the "stubborn old hag". She was sixty-three, but she looked ten years older than her actual age, dressed in clothing from the Wilson era when he entered the White House.
[40] She walked straight to the corpse covered with a thick blanket, her posture proud and judgmental, like a goddess of fate. Sergeant Sam noticed the man following her - a tall, tense, blond man with features similar to Mrs. Hate's - stammering something in her ear, but she ignored him and continued to the corpse, lifting the blanket, and staring unblinkingly at the broken, unrecognizable face.
[41] Sergeant Sam did not interfere, allowing her to immerse herself in her emotionless thoughts. He observed her face for a while, then turned to the men standing beside her. The tall, tense blond man - looking about thirty-two - was Konrad Hate, the only son of York and Emily Hate. Konrad resembled his mother in appearance, with a predatory look; but he was also weak and dissolute, with a sense of disillusionment. He seemed nervous, glancing quickly at the dead man's face and then looking down at the floor, his right foot beginning to twitch nervously.
[42] Standing next to him were two elderly men whom Sergeant Sam had already met during his investigation of the York Hate disappearance case. One was Dr. Milian, a tall, gray-haired man who appeared to be well over seventy, with thin shoulders. Dr. Milian examined the dead man's face without any signs of discomfort, but it was clear that he was uncomfortable, and Sergeant Sam assumed it was because he was an old acquaintance of the deceased. His companion was the most peculiar of the group - a cunning and not very honorable man, very thin and weak, Captain Kidd, a retired sailor and an old friend of the Hate family. Sergeant Sam was surprised to discover - he was flustered that he had not noticed before - that Captain Kidd had a wooden leg covered in leather under the right leg of his sailor's trousers. Captain Kidd's throat seemed to be choking on something, and he kept clearing his throat.
[43] He put a withered, weathered hand on Mrs. Hate's arm in a pleading gesture, but she shook it off - with a stiff arm, Captain Kidd blushed and stepped back.
[44] She finally took her eyes off the body: "This... I don't recognize it, Sergeant Sam."
[45] Sergeant Sam reached into his coat pocket and cleared his throat: "No, of course you don't, it's almost unrecognizable, Mrs. Hate... over here! Look at these clothes and belongings."
[46] The old lady nodded slightly, making a single emotional gesture as she followed Inspector Sam to the chair with the wet clothes - she licked her thin, red lips, as if a cat had just finished a delicious meal. Dr. Milian silently took her place beside the corpse, motioning for Conrad Blackthorn and Captain Quiver to step away, and then lifting the thick blanket covering the body. Dr. Shering watched with a skeptical professional eye.
[47] "These clothes are York's; he was wearing them the day he disappeared."
[48] "And, Blackthorn matriarch, these personal items." Inspector Sam led her to the table. She slowly picked up the seal ring with her fingers, her cloudy eyes sweeping over the pipe, wallet, keychain...
[49] "This is his," she said emotionlessly, "this ring, I gave him - what is this?" She suddenly became agitated, snatching up the note, reading it in an instant, and then nodding coldly and almost indifferently, "York's handwriting, it's not a forgery."
[50] Conrad walked over, listless, his eyes darting from one item to another, as if searching for a place to rest. He seemed to be stirred by the deceased's note: he rummaged through the pockets of the clothes, pulling out some documents, and muttered, "It's suicide, thought he didn't have the guts, old fool -"
[51] "Where are his handwriting samples?" the sergeant suddenly asked, a surge of anger rising within him.
[52] The blond son handed the papers to Sam, the sergeant bending over to review them with a touch of annoyance. Mrs. Harte neither looked at the corpse nor glanced at her husband's belongings, but began to straighten her thin shawl around her bony neck.
[53] "It's his handwriting, alright," the sergeant grumbled reluctantly. "Well, I suppose that's settled then." Despite this, he put the letter and other handwriting documents in his pocket. He looked at the corpse, Dr. Mireau pulling the shroud back over it. "What do you think, doctor? You knew him, is this Nak Harte?"
[54] The old doctor answered without looking at Sam, "I think so, yes, it is."
[55] "A man over sixty years old," Dr. Schelling unexpectedly spoke up. "Small hands and feet. An old appendectomy scar. Had surgery, probably for gallstones, about six or seven years ago. Right, doctor?"
[56] "Right, I operated on him myself eighteen years ago. And the other one – the bile duct gallstone, not a serious illness, performed by Dr. Robbins at Johns Hopkins Hospital... This is York Blackett."
[57] The old woman said, "Conrad, arrange the funeral. Not publicly. Send a brief statement to the press. No wreaths. Execute immediately.” She started walking towards the door, with Captain Kidd wobbling behind her, muttering something that sounded like an attempt to comply.
[58] "Wait a minute, Blackett's wife," Sergeant Sam said, and she stopped and turned to face him, "Don't go so fast, why did your husband commit suicide?"
[59] "I said, this –" Conrad stammered.
[60] "Conrad!" He retreated like a dog that had been hit. The old woman walked back to her place, standing so close to the sergeant that he could smell the faint acidic breath from her nose and mouth.
[61] "What do you want?" she said sharply and clearly. "Are you not satisfied with my husband's suicide?"
[62] Sergeant Sam was taken aback: "Oh, yes, of course."
[63] "Well, that's settled then. You're not to bother me any further," she said with a scowl and left. Captain Kemp seemed to breathe a sigh of relief and followed her out, stumbling a bit. Dr. Kemp swallowed and followed, looking pale and sick.
[64] "Well, that's that," said Dr. Thorn after the door had closed. "Now you know how to behave yourself." He chuckled. "Good Lord, what a woman!" He pushed the corpse onto the refrigeration shelf.
[65] Sergeant Sam cursed and stormed out the door.
[66] An alert young man outside caught his thick arm, and they began to walk briskly together.
[67] "Inspector! Hey, hey, good evening! I hear you found Black's body?"
[68] "Damn it," said Sam angrily.
[69] "Yes," the reporter replied enthusiastically. "I just saw her come out, strutting around like she owned the place. Cocky as all hell. Listen, Inspector, you're here for a reason, I know. Anything interesting going on?"
[70] "Let go of me, you little monkey!"
[71] "Still such a bad temper, dear Inspector... Can I say that there is a suspicion of illegality?"
[72] Sam put his hands in his pockets and looked down at his visitor: "You dare," he said, "I'll break every bone in your body. You bastards, never satisfied? Go away, it's a suicide!"
[73] "I thought the Inspector didn't agree——"
[74] "Get out of here! Evidence is clear. Now get out of here, kid, before I kick you out."
[75] He strode down the stairs of the morgue and hailed a taxi. The reporter watched him thoughtfully, the smile fading from his face.
[76] A man came running from the direction of Second Avenue, panting.
[77] "Hey, Jack!" he called, "Any new news on the Blackthorn case? Did you see the old witch?"
[78] The man who had been pestering Sam shrugged and watched the inspector's taxi drive away.
[79] "Answer your second question - I saw her, but nothing. Anyway, there's a follow-up article to be done... " He sighed, "Ah, whether it's murder or not, I can only say - thank God, let the crazy Blackthorn family exist!"
[80] Second Act
[81] Blackwood Manor, April 10th, Sunday, 2:30 PM
[82] The crazy Blackwood family...
[83] Many years ago, during a period of unusual Blackwood family news, an imaginative reporter took the name "Blackwood family" from the book "Alice's Adventures in Wonderland" as a nickname. Unfortunately, it may have been too exaggerated, as they were not half as crazy as the immortal Blackwood characters in the book, nor were they even a fraction as interesting. They were, in fact - as whispered by the fading neighbors of the square - "a bunch of annoying and annoying people". And although they were one of the oldest families in the area, they never had a sense of community, always keeping their distance from the prestigious families of Greenwich Village.
[84] The nickname stuck. They were always in the news, whether it was the blond Könrad drinking almost smashing an underground bar; or the clever Barbara leading a new poetry dance or hosting a cocktail party that critics raved about; or, of course, Giselle, the youngest of the three Blackwood children, who was beautiful, capricious, and had a nose for pleasure, with rumors of her addiction to opium and occasional stories of weekend wild parties in the Adirondack Mountains. And, every month, she made a very boring announcement of "engagement" with some wealthy young man - the catch being that the man was never from a respectable family.
[85] They were not only cut from the same mold, but all from the same odd, unconventional mold. Although each was so bizarre, dissolute, unpredictable, and unconventional, none could match their infamous mother. Emily was even crazier in her youth than her youngest daughter Giselle, and in middle age, she became tyrannical, stubborn, and domineering, and there was no social power she could not manipulate, and no market she could not exploit with her cunning, bloodthirsty, and gambling nature. There were rumors that her Wall Street investments had been devastated, causing her inherited wealth from several generations of wealthy, astute German ancestors to melt away like butter under her fiery gaze. Even her lawyers didn't know the exact number of her assets. In the post-war New York era, an era of gossip magazines, she was often referred to as "the richest woman in America" - clearly an unsubstantiated claim; and there were rumors that she was on the brink of bankruptcy, which was pure hearsay.
[86] Based on all of this – her family, her personal merits and demerits, her background, and her colorful history – Old Emily Hate is both the most hated and loved figure in the news industry. They hate her because she is an infuriating old hag; they love her because, as the editor-in-chief of a major newspaper put it, "With Mrs. Hate, there's always news."
[87] Many had predicted that York Heat would eventually take his own life before he jumped into the icy waters of the bay. Flesh and blood, they said – like the sincere and selfless York Heat – could only endure to such a limit, and beyond that, it would be unbearable. This man, in nearly forty years of life, had been treated like a dog and used like a horse. Under the sharp teeth of his wife, he had shriveled up, lost his personality, and become a ghost pursued day and night, first trapped in an environment that was reckless, unreasonable, cruel, and insane.
[88] His identity, from all accounts, is "Emily Black's husband" - at least since they held their colorful wedding in New York, which was thirty-seven years ago, when half-lion half-duck was the most popular motif on ornaments, and chair covers were still a must-have in living rooms. From the first day they returned to their Washington Square residence - to say, her house - York Black was aware of his fate. He was young then, perhaps he tried to resist her stubbornness, her explosive temper, and her tyranny. Perhaps he reminded her that she had divorced her first husband, Tom Cabbian, under some unknown circumstances; therefore, honestly speaking, she owed her second husband, York Black, some slight courtesies, and she should also tone down her inappropriate behavior that had shocked New York since her teenage years. Even if he tried, his fate was sealed, and it doomed his promising future.
[89] York Heat was once a chemist – though a young, poor, and inexperienced scientific novice – and also a researcher who had made groundbreaking new discoveries. During his marriage, he was conducting experiments with chemical gels, which were unimaginable in the Victorian-era chemical community. However, under the pressure of his wife, the chemical gels, his career, and his reputation all faded away. Year after year, he became increasingly melancholic, and eventually, he found solace only in the makeshift laboratory he was allowed to set up in his own room by Emily. He gradually became a shell of a man, relying on his wife's wealth to survive (and constantly reminded of this) and the father of a group of wayward children, but his control over them was less than that of the household staff.
[90] Barbara is the eldest of the Hotchkiss daughters and the most sensible of Emily's wild blood. She is a thirty-six-year-old spinster, tall and slender, with light golden hair, and she is the only one of the younger generation whose genes have not been corrupted; she loves all living things, especially nature, which sets her apart from her siblings. Out of the three Hotchkiss daughters, only she has inherited her father's talents, and she also inherits some of the abnormal elements from her mother, but on her, they become a highlight of genius and are expressed in her poetry. She is widely recognized as the leading female poet of her time - the literary world unquestionably calls her the poet of anarchy, a Bohemian with an independent spirit, and an intellectual with boundless poetic talent. She is the author of countless obscure, brilliant poetry collections, and with her melancholic, intelligent green eyes, she has become the priestess of the New York intellectual's Apollo temple.
[91] Barbara's younger brother, Konrad, lacked the artistic flair to balance his eccentricities. He was his mother's male counterpart, a classic Bates family madman. He had been on the dean's list of three different universities for his wicked and insane pranks, which had resulted in his expulsion from each institution. He had a history of two failed marriages that ended up in court. Once he drove recklessly and killed a pedestrian, only narrowly avoiding a conviction through his mother's lawyer's extensive bribery. There were countless instances of his Bates family temper flaring up due to abnormal blood alcohol levels, resulting in him lashing out at innocent bartenders. So far, he had broken his nose once (which had been set by a plastic surgeon), dislocated his collarbone, and accumulated an innumerable amount of stitches and bruises.
[92] However, he could not break free from his mother's iron will either. The elderly woman grabbed his nape and pulled him out of the chaos, arranging him to do business with a reliable and commendable young man named John Glee. This did not stop Conrad from continuing his associations with his meat-eating friends, and it was only through Glee's steady hand that their intermediary business remained intact.
[93] At a moment of relative clarity, he met and married an unfortunate young woman. Of course, the marriage did not correct his erratic lifestyle. His wife, Martha, a delicate young woman of his own age, soon realized the misfortune she faced. Forced to live under the oppressive shadow of her elderly mother-in-law, Blackthorn, she suffered her husband's abuse and neglect. Her once lively face soon took on a permanent look of fear. Like her father-in-law, York Blackthorn, she became a lost soul in this infernal realm.
[94] Poor Martha combined with the fickle Konrad, it was impossible to expect happiness; her only solace came from their two children, thirteen-year-old Jagger and four-year-old Billy... However, this was not without its share of mixed feelings. Jagger was a wild, impulsive, and precocious teenager, also a rough young boy with a knack for devising cruel pranks, not only on his mother but also on his aunts and grandparents. Billy, tired out, was a Martha, living each day was an endless struggle to clean up after them.
[95] As for Gigi Hedtke...as Barbara said: "She is the eternal social newcomer. She lives for sensation. Gigi is the most evil woman I know—double evil because she never keeps her promises with her beautiful lips and seductive gestures." - Gigi is 25 years old - "She is a tasteless orchid, a thoroughly despicable character." She sleeps around with men. Her constant catchphrase is: "Live it up!" In summary, Gigi is her mother's younger version.
[96] Most people would say that, just by hearing this, the family was already insane enough—a cold and hard-hearted matriarch, a withered and weakling forced to commit suicide, a genius Barbara, a playboy Konrad, an evil and heretical Ginger, a helpless and vulnerable Martha, and two unhappy children. However, there was still someone in this family who was so extraordinary, so tragic, and so pitiful that compared to her, the eccentricities of others were almost normal.
[97] That was Louise.
[98] She called herself Louise Kabbian because, although she was Emily's daughter, her father was not York Bates but Tom Kabbian, Emily's first husband. She was forty years old, petite, a bit overweight, and indifferent to the insane asylum she found herself in. She was mentally clear, mild-tempered, patient, and never complained, making her a likable woman. However, due to being surrounded by the infamous Bates family, she was not pushed to the background but became the most well-known figure in the Bates family. Even from the moment of her birth, she was treated as a tool for creating scandals, and her bad reputation and rumors followed her throughout her miserable and bizarre life.
[99] In fact, when Louise Kabbian was born to Emily and Tom Kabbian, she was born blind and deaf and had early symptoms of deafness, which the doctors said would worsen with age, eventually leading to complete deafness.
[100] The doctor's cruel prediction came true. On her eighteenth birthday—as if a birthday gift from the dark god who ruled her fate—Louise Kabbian faced the final torture of complete deafness.
[101] For anyone with insufficient willpower, this misfortune could be fatal. At the age of blooming, other girls were just beginning to explore the world of emotions and desires, while Louise was trapped in a world of only herself - a world without sound, images, and colors; a world without expression or expression. The last bridge connecting her to the world, her hearing, fell behind her, and the dark god swept it away without mercy. There was no turning back, and she faced denial, emptiness, and withered life. From the perspective of the sensory world, she might as well be dead.
[102] Although shaky, timid, and frightened, she did not become helpless, as there was something steel-like in her nature - perhaps an advantage inherited from her malignant mother - that made her strong and face her hopeless world with extraordinary courage. Even though she knew why she was so unfortunate, she never revealed it; and her relationship with her maker was no less than that of a normal mother and daughter.
[103] The cruel fact tells us that the daughter's misfortune was caused by her mother. When she was born, there was suspicion that her father, Tom Kibian, was the culprit, and that his bloodline was flawed and reaping the consequences on the child. But after Kibian divorced Emily and Emily remarried, giving birth to a group of devilish garbage in the insane Hate family, the world finally confirmed that the fault lay with the woman. At this time, it also strengthened the view that the fault lay with the woman, and Kibian had previously married once, and the son born from that marriage was normal. The news media quickly forgot Kibian, who died mysteriously a few years after divorcing Emily, and the son's whereabouts were unknown, while Emily, who was tightly clamped by the unfortunate York Hate, brought her first marriage's offspring into her mansion on Washington Square - a house destined to fall into a very painful and bitter tragedy; compared to what had happened before, it was probably just the weak prelude to this play.
[104] The tragic play began two months after York Hate's body was recovered from the bay.
[105] At first, there were no signs. Mrs. Hate's housekeeper and cook, Mrs. Abigail, served Louise Kibian a glass of egg-nog every afternoon after lunch as a routine. The egg-nog was just an old lady's show, and Louise was in good health except for a slightly weak heart and was actually not lacking in protein. But Mrs. Hate's orders could not be disobeyed, and Mrs. Abigail, as a servant, dared not say a word; and Louise, under her mother's iron grip, was also obedient enough to drink this cup of maternal nectar every afternoon. The importance of this long-term habit will become apparent in later events. Mrs. Abigail, who dared not dream of disobeying Mrs. Hate's orders, always placed the high glass of egg-nog on the southwest corner of the table, two inches away from the table edge - and
[106] Tragedy, or rather, a near tragedy, occurred on a mild Sunday in April, when everything seemed normal... until the incident took place.
[107] 2:20 PM - Inspector Sam carefully checked the exact time after the event - Mrs. Abuco prepared the egg-nog (she angrily revealed the ingredients to the police during their questioning), personally served it on a tray to the dining room, placing it on the southwest corner of the table, two inches from the edge, and then, with her duty done - left the dining room to return to the kitchen. She testified that when she entered the dining room, it was empty, and she did not see anyone come in while she was placing the egg-nog. At this point, everything was clear.
[108] Subsequent events are somewhat difficult to reconstruct, with the testimonies of the police not entirely precise. There was a chaotic period in which no one could objectively observe and describe the exact location, words, and sequence. Sergeant Sam had to make an imperfect inference that around 2:30, Lucy emerged from her bedroom with the iron-handed old lady and descended to the dining room for an egg and milk drink. They stopped in the hallway, and poet Barbara Hite, who accompanied them downstairs, also halted to watch, though she could not explain why, only that she vaguely sensed something was amiss. Meanwhile, Conrad's timid wife, Martha, appeared on the corridor with a worried expression, muttering, "Where has Jock gone? He was just trampling the flowers in the garden again." She also paused in the corridor to look around.
[109] Coincidentally, there was also a fifth eyewitness who peered into the dining room to observe the incident. This was Captain Quiver, the old sea dog, a neighbor of the Blacketts, who had accompanied the old lady and Conrad to the morgue two months ago to identify the body. Captain Quiver appeared in the second of the two corridors leading to the dining room - not the one that leads to the main entrance, but the one that leads to the library adjoining the dining room.
[110] The initial scene they witnessed was nothing out of the ordinary. Martha's eldest son, the thirteen-year-old shorty named Jodi, was alone in the restaurant, holding the glass of eggnog in his hand and staring intently into the cup. The old lady glared with open eyes, barking a sharp reprimand, which caused Jodi to turn guiltily, immediately noticing the audience. His mischievous face twisted into a grimace, and a determined expression of playfulness flashed in his wild eyes as he raised the glass to his lips and quickly swallowed a mouthful of the cream.
[111] What followed was a commotion. In an instant - just as his grandmother rushed up to him, slapping his hand fiercely and screeching, "You know it's Lucy's, you little scoundrel! I've told you many times not to steal her things!" - Jody dropped the cup, his face turning pale with shock. The glass cup shattered on the floor, and the egg-nog spilled all over the tiles in the dining room. Then, with both hands covering his mouth, he began to scream.
[112] Jody's slender and stubborn body began to convulse, his hands spasmed, and his pain intensified, his breathing becoming labored. His face turned an odd shade of gray, and he screamed, collapsing onto the floor.
[113] A piercing shriek echoed through the house, and Mrs. Abuco rushed in, followed by her husband, George Abuco - the housekeeper and driver; and the tall, thin old maid, Vichneya; and Kronrad, who had been drinking heavily since early Sunday and was now disheveled and flushed. Helpless Lucy was left behind, standing in the hallway, bewildered. She seemed to sense something was wrong from her sixth sense, so she stumbled forward, sniffing the air, searching for her mother's location, and then grabbed the old lady's arm in terror.
[114] As expected, Mrs. Blackt was the first to recover from the shock of Jody's convulsions and Martha's fainting. She jumped to Jody's side, dragging the dazed Martha away, lifting Jody's neck - by now his face was purple - and prying open his stiff jaw with her bony finger. He coughed, then vomited.
[115] Not surprisingly, Mrs. Blackt was the first to recover from the shock of Jody's convulsions and Martha's fainting. She jumped to Jody's side, dragging the dazed Martha away, lifting Jody's neck - by now his face was purple - and prying open his stiff jaw with her bony finger. He coughed, then vomited.
[116] Her garnet eyes lit up: "Abigail! Call Dr. Millian immediately!" she shouted. George Abigail ran out of the restaurant. Mrs. Blackthorn's eyes darkened again, and she repeated the first aid measures without hesitation, and the boy vomited again.
[117] Except for Captain Cuddy, everyone else seemed paralyzed, just staring at the old lady and the writhing boy. Captain Cuddy nodded approvingly at Mrs. Blackthorn's strong response before walking away to find the deaf and blind woman. Lousia felt him touch her soft shoulder, as if recognizing who it was, and reached into his palm to hold his hand.
[118] However, the most crucial part of this scene took place without anyone noticing. A spotted-eared dog - the pet of little Billy - had sneaked into the restaurant unnoticed. Upon seeing the spilled eggnog, he happily ran up to it, his little nose buried in the milk.
[119] The maid Vicky suddenly screamed, pointing at the dog.
[120] The dog convulsed weakly on the ground. He trembled and spasmed a few times. Then his four legs stiffened, his belly only suddenly swelled, and he lay still. It was clear that the dog would no longer enjoy the eggnog.
[121] Doctor Miller, who lived nearby, arrived within five minutes. He wasted no time on the stunned Black family, barely glancing at them, and the old doctor obviously knew his patients well.
[122] He glanced at the stiff dog and the trembling boy and frowned: "Send him upstairs immediately. You, Conrad, help me carry him out." At this point, Conrad, whose eyes were already clear, looked terrified and picked up his son, leaving the dining room with Doctor Miller, who had opened his medical bag.
[123] Barbara mechanically knelt down and began rubbing Martha's numb hands; Mrs. Black remained silent, her wrinkled face as hard as rock.
[124] Clad in a kimono and still sleepy, Gigi rushed into the dining room: "What's going on?" She yawned, "I saw Doctor Miller and Conrad and the little monster upstairs…" She widened her eyes and stopped talking when she saw the stiff dog, the spilled egg and milk, the unconscious Martha, "How…" No one paid attention to her, and no one answered. She sat down in a chair, staring at her sister-in-law's pale face.
[125] A middle-aged, tall, and plump woman in white clothes walked in - this was Lucy's nurse, Miss Smith. She looked around and her kindly face immediately showed terror. She looked at Mrs. Black, who stood motionless like a granite statue; she looked at Lucy, who stood trembling beside Captain Kidd; and then she sighed, waved Barbara away, and knelt down to tend to the unconscious woman in a professional manner.
[126] No one spoke. They seemed to be struck by the same nerve, all turning and looking at the old lady, but Blackthorn's face remained inscrutable. At this point, she had one arm around Lulu's trembling shoulders and was watching Smith's quick movements in tending to Martha with a blank expression.
[127] After what seemed like an eternity, there was some movement. They heard Dr. Mirean's heavy footsteps as he descended the stairs. He slowly walked in, put down his medical bag, glanced at Martha, who was gradually regaining consciousness under Smith's care, and nodded. He turned to Blackthorn's wife.
[128] "Jody is out of danger, Blackthorn's wife," he said calmly. "Thank you, quick thinking. The amount he swallowed was not fatal, and the immediate vomiting undoubtedly saved him from serious illness. He'll be fine."
[129] Blackthorn's wife nodded haughtily, then raised her chin, staring at the old doctor with an interested yet cold and distant expression. She heard a serious undertone in his voice. But Dr. Mirean moved away, first checking the dead dog, then sniffing the liquid on the ground, finally taking a small vial from his bag, screwing on the lid, and putting it away. He stood up and whispered something to Nurse Smith, who nodded and left the dining room. They heard her heading upstairs to the nursery, where Jody was lying in bed moaning.
[130] Then Dr. Mirean bent down to Martha, helping her stand up, and calmly reassured her – the silence around them was as still as a graveyard – and a strange but not cowardly expression flashed across the weak woman's face. She swayed as she left the dining room, following Smith upstairs to the nursery. She passed her husband on the way, neither speaking, and Konrad stumbled into the dining room and sat down.
[131] As if she had been waiting for this moment, and as if Kronrad's entrance was a signal, Miss Bates slammed her palm on the table with a force that startled everyone, except Lucy, who only huddled deeper into the old lady's embrace.
[132] "Well!" Miss Bates exclaimed, "By all that's holy, now let's get to the bottom of this. Dr. Mirian, what's in that eggnog that made the brat such a mess?"
[133] Dr. Mirian whispered, "Strychnine."
[134] "Poison, huh? I knew it, just look at that dog." Miss Bates seemed to grow several inches taller, scanning the room. "I'm going to get to the bottom of this, you ungrateful bastards!"
[135] Barbara sighed and placed her delicate fingers on the back of a chair, leaning against it.
[136] Her mother continued in a chilling tone, "That eggnog was Lucy's. Lucy drinks it at the same time and place every day, you all know. Someone must have put poison in it while Mrs. Abigail was setting it on the table, between the time the brat came in and took the cup. It's clear that they knew Lucy would drink it!"
[137] "Mom," said Barbara, "let it go."
[138] "Shut up! Jocky saved Lucy's life, almost lost his own. My poor Lucy is safe and sound, but the fact remains that someone wanted to poison her!" Mrs. Blackett hugged the deaf, dumb, and blind woman to her chest, and Lucy made unintelligible sobbing sounds. "It's okay, it's okay, dear," the old lady comforted her, as if Lucy could hear, stroking her daughter's hair and then speaking in a sharp tone again, "Who put the poison in the eggnog?"
[139] Gigi scoffed, "Don't be so dramatic, Mom."
[140] Conrad weakly said, "What are you talking about, Mom? We wouldn't—"
[141] "Who? All of you! You all hate seeing her! My poor, miserable Lou..." Mrs. Hotchkiss tightened her embrace around her daughter, "What?!" she seethed, her old bones shaking with anger, "Say it! Who did it?"
[142] "Dr. Merrill," Dr. Merrill spoke up.
[143] Her anger dissipated instantly, her eyes now filled with suspicion, "I'll ask for your opinion when I want it, Dr. Merrill. Don't interrupt!"
[144] "That," Dr. Merrill replied coldly, "I'm afraid it's not possible."
[145] She narrowed her eyes, "What do you mean?"
[146] "I mean," Dr. Mirena replied, "I have a duty, and this is a criminal case, Mrs. Blackthorn. I have no choice."
[147] The old lady's mouth hung open, her face turning as purple as Jock's, as she shoved Lulu aside and strode forward, grabbing Dr. Mirena's shoulders and shaking him vigorously.
[148] "No, you can't!" she screamed, "Oh, no, you can't, you meddling fool! Are you going to expose it? The more it's exposed, the more—Don't touch the phone, Mirena! Look at me—"
[149] Despite the old woman's frantic tugging at his arm and her curses falling on his white head, Dr. Mirena remained calmly raising the telephone receiver.
[150] Dr. Mirena dialed the police headquarters.

[152] The first act
[153] A murder case, though lacking a tongue, can still speak through its most extraordinary organs.
[154] First scene
[155] Hamlet Manor, April 17, Sunday, 12:30 PM
[156] Detective Sam had an interest in thinking that, at first, God created the earth, and he indeed had a good job, especially when he was miles away from the metropolis, in the Hudson River area of Westchester County, where he felt a special touch in his heart.
[157] Due to his heavy duty as a police officer, Detective Sam rarely had the opportunity to have religious or aesthetic thoughts, but even with his busy schedule, he couldn't be indifferent to the surrounding beauty.
[158] His car struggled up a narrow path, moving forward as if climbing to the sky, and what he saw was a heavenly world of ramparts, fortresses, green towers, and a blue sky, with the shimmering waters of the Hudson River and white sails dotting the blue waves far below. Detective Sam inhaled the air deeply, filled with the fragrance of wood, pine, and sweet flowers, under the bright sun and the refreshing April breeze that brushed his gray hair. As he turned a curve on the road, Detective Sam pieced together an eloquent sentence, thinking that even with the crime, the beauty still made him feel alive. This was his sixth visit to Phil Rye's impressive residence - Hamlet Manor, and as he thought, this remarkable place kept him captivated each time.
[159] He stopped his car at a familiar small bridge - the gate of Phil Rye's estate - and waved to the guard like a little boy, a smiling little old man holding an old bridge latch.
[160] "Hey!" Sam called out, "Nice weather, can I go to Mr. Rye's house?"
[161] "Yes, sir," the gatekeeper replied loudly, "Yes, sir. Come in, Inspector, Mr. Rheingold said you could come anytime. This way, please!" He jumped onto the bridge and pulled open a creaking gate, indicating for the inspector to drive his car over the quaint wooden bridge.
[162] Inspector Sam sighed contentedly and stepped on the accelerator. What a beautiful day!
[163] The terrain was familiar - a perfect gravel path, a turning green shrubbery, and suddenly, like a delightful dream, a grassy expanse spread out before the castle. The castle, standing majestically hundreds of feet high on a cliff overlooking the Hudson River, was Mr. J. Rheingold's masterpiece. The design had been harshly criticized by contemporary critics, who dismissed it as the work of an "outdated traditionalist," "behind the times," and "pretentious" - the last remark coming from a sharp-tongued theater critic.
[164] But - look at the castle, surrounded by meticulously tended gardens, neatly arranged yews, Elizabethan-style cottages, cobblestones, footpaths, moats, drawbridges, and the imposing giant itself, built of stone on stone. It was the essence of the 16th century, a part of old England, and it was drawn from the plays of Shakespeare... It was the natural setting for the retired gentleman's opulent accomplishments.
[165] Even the most scathing critics could not deny that he had made a great contribution to the timeless Shakespearean stage, and his near-genius performances had brought him great wealth, fame, and private joy. So, this was the abode of the retired Emperor of the Theater, Mr. J. Rheingold. As another elderly man opened the heavy iron gates surrounding the estate, Inspector Sam thought to himself, for all the dullards in New York, this was peace, beauty, and a place to escape the hustle and bustle of the city.
[166] He suddenly slammed on the brakes, and the car came to a halt with a screech. Twenty feet to his left, there was a startling sight – a stone elf Ariel fountain in the center of a bed of tulips… The Chief Officer was mesmerized by the strange man splashing water in the pool with his rough, brown hand.
[167] The hunchbacked old man looked up, his small, cunning eyes flashing.
[168] "Hey, you, Quix!" the Chief Officer exclaimed, "What are you doing?"
[169] Quix is a major figure in Joram Rine, Esq.'s illustrious history – he has been his wig and makeup artist for forty years – and he placed his two small hands on his thin, bent hips.
[170] "I was observing a goldfish," he answered seriously in his short, broken voice, "Welcome, Chief Officer Sam!"
[171] Sam stepped out of the car and stretched. "I don't come around much, do you feel well, old man?"
[172] Quincannon reached into the water with one hand, which wriggled and held a small, squirming thing above the surface.
[173] "Such beautiful colors," he observed, his thin lips clicking. "You mean Mr. Philo Vance? Oh, he's doing fine. I'm sorry, old man? He's younger than you, Sergeant Sam, you know, sixty years old, Mr. Vance, but he runs faster than you, like a – like a rabbit, and he swam four miles in that – it's freezing – that icy lake this morning. Can you do that?"
[174] "I don't think so," the sergeant smiled, carefully avoiding stepping on the tulip beds as he walked. "Where is he?"
[175] The goldfish lost its courage and suddenly stopped wriggling, and the old hunchback almost regretfully dropped it back into the water. "Behind those privet hedges, they're pruning them. He's very particular about the aesthetics, I mean Mr. Vance. These gardeners like –"
[176] Sam pushed aside a trimmed privet hedge, from behind which came the sound of busy snipping and the distinctive deep, pleasant voice of Reine. He stepped through the bushes, smiling at a tall, thin man in striped pants surrounded by gardeners.
[177] "Mr. Jules Reine himself, in person," the officer announced as he extended a giant hand, "Well, well! How do you stay so young?"
[178] "Chief!" Reine exclaimed happily, "What a surprise! I'm so glad to see you!"
[179] He dropped a heavy pair of pruning shears and shook Sam's hand. "How did you find me? Most people take hours to find me at Hamlet Manor."
[180] "Quincannon told me," the officer said, eagerly collapsing onto the lush grass, "Ah—ah! Wonderful! He's back there in the water garden."
[181] "You're right," Sam grumbled. "Sorry, no chance to argue. You look like a violin in good shape."
[182] He looked enviously at his partner. Rayne was tall and thin and looked vigorous, with a head of silver hair that reached his neck. He looked younger than sixty, with a face that was as youthful as his features. His gray-green eyes were shrewd and deep, with no trace of age. His throat was strong and tanned from the sun. His face was firm and steady, yet able to change in an instant, a mature and strong face of a man. Even his voice was authoritative and resonant, capable of wielding words like a sword, and to many listeners, it was incredibly sexy. In short, he was a remarkable man.
[183] "You have something to say," Philip Leander Rayne said, his eyes flashing. "You didn't come all the way from the city without a reason. It's simple to deduce that you've forgotten me all winter - in fact, since the Longsword case, you haven't been here. What's on your mind?"
[184] His sharp gaze fixed on the officer's lips. The actor had lost his hearing due to this late-life change, which forced him to retire from the theater. With his remarkable adaptability to new things, he quickly learned lip reading, and his ability to read lips was so good that most people who met him had no idea of his impairment.
[185] Sam looked embarrassed: "Don't say that, don't say that, Rayne. In fact, something happened in New York that has me confused. Maybe you'd like to take a crack at it."
[186] "A crime," the old actor pondered, "not the Blackett case, I suppose?"
[187] The commissioner's eyes lit up: "So you've read the newspaper report! Yes, it's that crazy Blackett family. Someone tried to poison the daughter of the first wife - Louisa Cabian."
[188] "The one who is deaf, mute, and blind," Rine said solemnly, "I'm particularly interested in her, commissioner, she's an outstanding example of humanity's ability to overcome physical disabilities... Obviously, you haven't cracked the case yet."
[189] "Yes," the commissioner said angrily, grabbing a handful of grass from the ground, "no progress at all, no leads."
[190] Rine focused on him: "I've read the newspaper report," he said, "maybe some details were distorted or omitted. Anyway, I know some about this family and the egg, milk, and poison incident, and the near tragedy involving the children - all the surface facts." He stood up abruptly, "Have you had lunch, commissioner?"
[191] Sam scratched his freshly shaved blue chin: "Er... I'm not very hungry..."
[192] "What nonsense!" Rain grabbed Sam's muscular arm and lifted him slightly off the ground. The inspector was surprised to find himself partially lifted off the ground. "Come on, don't be shy. Let's have something to eat first, then a cold beer, and then we'll discuss your problem. You like beer, don't you?"
[193] Sam struggled to his feet, looking hungry: "I can't say I like it, but I don't want to say I don't..."
[194] "I knew it," Rain said enthusiastically. "My dear fellow, you really know how to get to my heart, Raines! Maybe we can convince my steward, Falstaff, to give us a drop or two, say, Martellus Triple Sec?"
[195] "Absolutely not!" the inspector said excitedly. "My goodness, you really do know me, Mr. Raines!"
[196] Mr. Rene strolled along the path lined with flowers, amused by his guest's excitement.
[197] They walked through the woods surrounding the castle, passing by red-roofed houses and cobblestone streets, narrow alleys, and pointed towers and gabled roofs, all of which were enchanting. The sergeant was so mesmerized that he almost forgot about the time until he saw a few people dressed in 20th-century clothing. Although he had visited Hamlet Manor several times before, this was his first time in the village.
[198] They stopped in front of a low, brown building with shuttered windows and a swinging sign.
[199] "Have you heard of the Mermaid Tavern? It's where Shakespeare, Ben Jonson, Robert, Francis, Bunbury, and others used to gather?"
[200] "I think I've heard of it," the sergeant said uncertainly. "A place where guys used to hang out and party in London."
[201] "Exactly," said Vance, bowing. "It's located in Cheapside, near Fleet Street. You can find as many classical and refined names there as you would on a Sunday at church. This," Vance continued, "is the faithful reproduction of that immortal tavern, Inspector. Let's go in."
[202] Inspector Sam smiled. The room with a wooden-beamed ceiling was filled with smoke, chatter, and the strong smell of good beer. He nodded approvingly. "If those boys from centuries ago went to a place like this, Mr. Vance, I'd agree with you. Hmm!"
[203] A rosy-cheeked, round-bellied, beer-barrel-shaped man with a white apron tied tightly around his waist rushed over to greet them.
[204] "Do you remember Falstaff, my unparalleled Falstaff?" asked Vance, patting the little old man's bald head.
[205] "Of course I do!"
[206] Falstaff—Falstaff—smiled and saluted: "A large beer, Mr. Vance?"
[207] "Yes, and a glass for Inspector Sam, too, and a bottle of brandy, and some good food. Come along, Inspector."
[208] He led them through the crowded room, nodding and smiling at the noisy customers. They found a corner at an empty table on a long bench like a church pew. The innkeeper, Falstaff, who was more than efficient, not only supervised the preparation of a delicious lunch but also served it himself. Inspector Sam took a deep breath and buried his ugly nose in the beer foam.
[209] "Now, Inspector," said the old actor when Sam had finished his last bite and drained the last drop of brandy from the bottle, "tell me where the difficulty lies."
[210] "The difficulty is here," the inspector grumbled. "There's nothing to tell you. If you read the papers, you know as much as I do. You've seen the news about the old lady's husband committing suicide a few months ago?"
[211] "Yes, the newspapers were full of reports of York's betrayal of his family. Tell me, what happened when you arrived at the scene."
[212] "Well," said Sergeant Saunders, leaning back in his high-backed oak chair, "the first thing I did was to investigate when the strychnine was added to the eggnog. Mrs. Abigail, the cook and housekeeper, placed the glass on the table in the dining room at about 2:25 p.m., and I estimate that five to ten minutes later, Blackthorn and her deaf, blind, and mute daughter came in and found the little scoundrel, Jockey, drinking his aunt's drink. That's as far as I can see."
[213] "Well, I pointed out to the reporters that anyone could have had the opportunity to poison the drink," said Rains. "Did you ask the child when he entered the dining room?"
[214] "Of course I did, but you know how children are. What can you do? He said he had just gone in before his grandmother and his Aunt Louisa saw him. We haven't been able to find out who might have gone into the dining room before the child."
[215] "Well, is the child completely recovered?"
[216] "How could he not be?" sniffed the Inspector. "It would take more than one dose of strychnine to kill him. What a brat! I felt like strangling him. He didn't mean to steal the bad egg-nog—oh, no, of course not! He didn't know why he drank the drink. He said, 'Emily Grandmother startled me, so I drank it down.' That's all. Luckily, he didn't drink more of it."
[217] "I'll bet you weren't a model child yourself, Inspector," said Lanyon with a smile. "During the time the eggnog was being poisoned, where were the others? The newspapers didn't make it clear."

[219] "Young ladies often go out the night before, I believe," said Lanyon without emotion. "I think she was at one of those so-called bacchanalian parties. An odd one, I must say. What about the others?"
[220] "Well, this Miss Lewis—a strange one—usually takes a nap after lunch, she and her mother sleep in the same room upstairs. Miss Black, the poet—she's the young scoundrel's sister—was writing in her upstairs studio. Miss Smith, Miss Lewis's nurse, had her room next to Miss Lewis's, overlooking the side alley—she said she was reading the Sunday newspaper."
[221] "And the rest?""
[222] "A few servants. Mrs. Abbot, the housekeeper - she was in the kitchen at the back, helping Viola with the dishes from lunch. Mr. Abbot, her husband, was in the garage at the back, polishing the car. That's about it, it doesn't look too promising, does it?"
[223] Rennie nodded, his eyes fixed on the inspector's lips. "The one-legged captain Kidd, you said," he finally spoke. "What role did he play in this, Inspector? What was he doing in the house at two thirty on Sunday afternoon?"
[224] "Oh, him," Sam grumbled. "He used to be a captain, had lived next to the Blackthorns for years after retiring - bought the house with his own money, we checked him out, don't worry - sailed his own ship for thirty years, until a storm in the South Atlantic forced him to retire. The waves knocked him over - he got some bad injuries on his leg, the first mate didn't take care of it properly, and by the time they reached the port, there was only a stump left, an old salt with a lot of experience."

[227] "Very likely, a physical disability can certainly help build friendship, so this kind-hearted captain was waiting to visit Louisa Kidd?"
[228] "Exactly. He visits her every day, they get along well, even the old witch approves - she's happy that someone actually cares about her deaf and mute daughter - who knows if anyone else does."
[229] "Constable, how do they communicate? After all, the poor woman can't hear, see, or speak."
[230] "Well, they have some ways," the constable says glumly, "You know, she didn't go completely deaf until she was ten, and they taught her a lot of things, but mostly, Captain Kidd just sits down and holds her hand, she likes him."
[231] "Poor thing! Well, Inspector, about the poison itself, have you traced the source of the strychnine?"
[232] Sam chuckled, "No luck there. We held onto that lead right from the start, but it turned out like this, you know, York Blackett never lost his passion for chemistry - from what I know, he was a renowned chemist in his youth, he set up a laboratory in his house and spent all day there."
[233] "It makes sense that the strychnine came from the laboratory, doesn't it?"
[234] Sam shrugged, "I suppose so, but we ran into trouble there too. After Blackett disappeared, the old lady locked the laboratory, strictly ordering that no one was allowed to go there, as if it were a kind of memorial to him - she wanted to keep the room as it was when he left - especially after two months when his body was found and it was confirmed that he was dead, you understand? There's only one key, and she always carries it with her, there's no other entrance to the laboratory - the windows are all barred with iron, you see, as soon as I heard about the laboratory, I rushed to take a look, and -"
[235] "You asked Mrs. Blackett for the key?"
[236] "Yes."
[237] "She always carries it with her, you're sure?”
[238] "She claims so. Anyway, we found a bottle of strychnine pills on the shelf where Blackthorn had nailed them, so we thought the poison came from that bottle—it's easier to drop a pill into an egg-nog than to bring powder or solution. But how did he get into the lab?”
[239] Rayne didn't answer right away, using a long, slender, and strong white finger to gesture to Falstaff. "Another round of beer... Detective, this is a rhetorical question. The windows are barred—someone must have been jealous of Blackthorn's refuge—the door is locked, and the only key is always on Blackthorn's wife, huh... It doesn't necessarily require a magical explanation, there's such a thing as a wax mold."
[240] "Of course," Sam snorted. "How could we not think of it? Rayne, I suggest there are three possibilities. First, the person who poisoned him may have stolen the strychnine from the lab before York Blackthorn disappeared, when the room was open to anyone, and the poison was kept until last Sunday…"
[241] "Very clever," Raine commented, "continue, Inspector."
[242] "Second, as you said, someone made a wax model of the lock and made a copy of the key, so they could get in and out of the lab and get the poison just before the crime."
[243] "Or a long time ago, Inspector, right?"
[244] "Or third, the poison was taken from an external source." Sam took a cup of overflowing beer from Faustaf and drank it down, "Great," he hiccupped, "I mean the beer. We've done everything we can on that. As for the key theory - the next step - we've been checking all the locksmiths and hardware stores... we haven't found anything yet. As for external sources - we're still investigating, and so far, we haven't found anything. That's the situation."
[245] Raine tapped his desk thoughtfully. The guests were thinning out, and there were only the two of them left in the Mermaid Tavern: "Have you thought," he said after a silence, "that the eggnog may have been poisoned before Mrs. Abigail brought it to the restaurant?"
[246] "Oh, Mother of God, Mr. Rene," the Sergeant growled, "Do you think I'm an idiot? I've checked the kitchen, and there's no trace of strychnine or poison.
