So it's possible that someone slipped in and put poison in the drink while Mrs. Abigail wasn't looking."
[247] "I'm beginning to sympathize with your predicament," Rene smiled sympathetically, "and I'm starting to agree with you, Sergeant. Was there anyone else in Blackthorn Manor on that Sunday afternoon?"
[248] "According to my investigation, no, but the front door was unlocked, so anyone could have come and gone without being noticed. Everyone who knows the Blackthorns knows about the egg-nog at 2:30 pm every Sunday afternoon."
[249] "I know that there's another person who was not at home at the time of the incident - the tutor of the Blackthorn children, Wendegast Pere. Have you investigated him?"
[250] "Of course. Pere was off on Sunday, he said he went for a long walk in Central Park all day - alone, until very late in the afternoon when he returned to the house, and I was already there."
[251] "How did he react to the news of the attempted poisoning?"
[252] "He seemed surprised and concerned, I would say, after he understood the situation. He couldn't provide any explanation."
[253] "We seem to be,” Detective Reine's deep-set features showed a fading smile, his eyebrows furrowed, "in a deeper fog. As for the motive, that's where the answer might lie."
[254] Detective Sam growled like a mighty bull, "Every one of those bastards could have a motive. The Blackthorns are all nuts – really like a bunch of madmen, the whole family, maybe except for the poetess, Barbara, even she's a bit off, just in a poet's way. You know, Mrs. Blackthorn's whole life revolves around her deaf, mute, and blind daughter, like a mother tiger watching over her cub, to the point of feeding her and dressing her – giving everything to avoid any trouble for Louisa, that's probably the only humane thing about that vicious old cat."
[255] "And the rest of the children, of course, are bound to be jealous," Detective Reine's bright eyes glinted, "yes, indeed. Emotional outbursts, wild temperament, and a violent tendency without moral guidance – we're starting to see the possibilities."
[256] "I spotted it a week ago," the inspector snorted, "All of Mrs. Blackthorn's attention is on Lulu, and the other children are jealous to death. It's not about sweetness, warmth, and 'I love you, dear mommy' type of emotions," the inspector sneered, "I doubt there's any love there, just pride and a competitive spirit. And, as for Lulu—remember, she's not their biological sister, Mr. J. B. Fletcher, they are half-siblings."
[257] "That makes a big difference," Mr. J. B. Fletcher agreed.
[258] "A big difference indeed," the inspector said. "For example, the youngest child, Jill, doesn't want anything to do with Lulu, claiming that Lulu's presence casts a shadow over the family, and her friends don't like to visit because Lulu's appearance makes everyone uncomfortable. That appearance! Was it intentional? But to Jill, it made no difference. If she were my daughter," the inspector slapped his thigh, "and Conrad too—he's always nagging his mother to send Lulu to a sanitarium so she doesn't interfere with their normal lives, saying she disrupts their normal lives. Normal! The only normal thing Conrad has is a case of the vapors."
[259] "And what about Barbara Blackthorn?"
[260] "That's another matter," Inspector Sam seemed to have a soft spot for the poetess, he took a swig of beer, licked his lower lip, and answered Mr. J. B. Fletcher in a tender tone, "My point is—she's a good girl, Mr. J. B. Fletcher, sensible. I'm not saying she loves the deaf girl, but from what I've observed, Barbara sympathizes with her and tries to make her life more interesting—which is what a truly good-hearted woman should do."
[261] "Miss Hight obviously captured another heart," Rain said, getting up, "Come on, Inspector, let's take a breath of fresh air."
[262] Sam struggled to get up, loosened his belt, and then led his master into the narrow and quaint street. They walked back to the garden, Rain lost in thought, his eyes unfocused, his lips tightly closed, and Sam looked gloomy and followed him step by step.
[263] "I think Conrad and his wife don't get along well," Rain finally spoke, sitting down on an ancient rusty bench, "Sit down, Inspector."
[264] Sam sat down, looking like a tired thinker, weak and powerless: "They don't get along, cat and dog living separate lives. She told me that once she had the chance, she would take the two children away from 'this terrible house' - she was very emotional, she really - I got some interesting information about Louise from her nurse, Miss Smith. A few weeks ago, Martha and the old lady had a disagreement, it seemed that Mrs. Black hit the two children, Martha was furious and cursed her grandmother-in-law as 'an evil old witch', accused her of meddling, and wished she would die - you know how women get when they're emotional. In short, the two were on the verge of a sword fight, Miss Smith pulled the children out of the room - the two children were stunned... Martha is usually as timid as a sheep, you know, but when provoked, she can be very fierce, I feel sorry for her, she lives in a 'mental institution', and I wouldn't let my children grow up in such an environment."
[265] "And Mrs. Black is a rich woman," Rain seemed to have been listening to Sam's story, lost in thought, "There may be financial motives behind it... " His expression grew increasingly gloomy.
[266] They sat in silence. The garden was chilly, and the sound of laughter drifted from the nearby village. The Inspector folded his arms across his chest and stared at Lanyon's face, clearly unsatisfied with what he saw. He growled, "Well, what's your verdict, Lanyon? Any clues?"
[267] Mr. Philo Vance sighed, managing a weak smile and shaking his head. "I'm afraid I'm not superhuman, Inspector."
[268] "You mean –"
[269] "I mean, I can't see anything. Who put the poison in the egg-nog? I can't even make a plausible theory. Evidence, evidence – it's not enough to form a clear hypothesis."
[270] Sam looked crestfallen; what he feared most was this outcome: "Any suggestions?"
[271] Raine shrugged. "Just one warning. Once a killer, always a killer. There will be another attempt on Miss Caplan's life, undoubtedly. Of course, not immediately, but eventually, when the killer thinks he's safe..."
[272] "We'll do our best to prevent it," the chief said, his tone lacking confidence.
[273] The old actor suddenly stood up, startling Sam. He looked up at Raine, who remained expressionless – clearly something was on his mind. "Chief, I heard that Dr. Millian took some samples from the poisoned eggnog on the restaurant floor?"
[274] The chief sighed in relief. "Oh, yes," he said. "I had Dr. Schell examine it at the city lab."
[275] "Did Dr. Schell report his findings?"
[276] "Well, well!" the inspector says, "What's the matter? It's not mysterious at all, Mr. Rene, of course he reported the results."
[277] "Did he say whether the poison in the egg-nog milk was a lethal dose?"
[278] The inspector sniffs: "Lethal? What do you mean? The doctor said the dose in the drink was enough to kill half a dozen people."
[279] A moment later, Rene's expression returns to its usual cheerful demeanor, with just a hint of disappointment lingering on his face, and the inspector reads defeat in his green-gray eyes.
[280] "So, the best advice I can give you - for your long journey's pitiful reward, Inspector!" Mr. Jury Rene says, "is that you really need to keep a close eye on the mad Blackthorn family."
[281] Scene Two
[282] Looie's bedroom, June 5th, Sunday, 10:00 a.m.
[283] From the very beginning, the Blackthorn case moved at a leisurely pace. It was not a series of crimes that blazed like a fire, nor a string of events that dazzled the eye, nor a frenzied type. It was very, very slow, almost walking at a lazy pace, and because of its slowness, it had a cruel, ominous feeling, like a funeral procession.
[284] In some ways, the slowness of the case's evolution had its importance, but at the time, including Mr. Raine, no one noticed or even suspected it. Mr. York Blackthorn disappeared in December, his body was found in February, someone tried to poison the deaf, mute, and blind woman in April, and then, almost two months later, on a bright Sunday morning in June...
[285] Raine comfortably settled into his castle on the Hudson River, long since forgetting about the Blackthorn case and the visit of Inspector Sam. The enthusiasm of the press for the poisoning case gradually subsided, and eventually the newspapers stopped mentioning the whole incident altogether, despite Inspector Sam's best efforts to find further clues to point out who might have been the poisoner. The excitement subsided, and the police investigation followed suit.
[286] Until June 5th.
[287] Mr. Philo Vance received the notification from the phone while lying naked on the empty city wall of the castle, with old Quincannon struggling to climb up the tower stairs, his ghostly face turning purple with exertion.
[288] "Inspector Sam..." he gasped, "He... He...”
[289] Mr. Vance sat up alertly: "What is it, Quincannon?"
[290] "He said," the old man panted, "something happened at the Blackthorn's house!"
[291] Renn's brown body leaned forward, bending his waist: "It's finally come," he said slowly, "When? Who? What did the Sergeant say?"
[292] Quincannon wiped the sweat from his forehead: "He didn't say, he was so excited, Sergeant was really shouting at me, I've never been—"
[293] "Quincannon!" Renn stood up, "Speak up."
[294] "Yes, Mr. Renn. He said if you wanted to know the situation, you should go to the Blackthorn's right away. He said, on North Washington Square, he would keep everything intact for you, but to hurry, he said!"
[295] Renn had already rushed down the spiral staircase.
[296] Two hours later, under the control of the young driver named Dromio, Ren's black Lincoln limousine weaved through the busy traffic on Fifth Avenue. As they passed Eighth Street, Ren could see the crowd gathered at Washington Square, with police officers maintaining order and the highway under the bridge blocked. Two motorcycle police officers blocked Dromio's path.
[297] "Don't come this way!" one of the police officers shouted, "Turn around and go the other way!"
[298] A plump, red-faced officer ran up: "Mr. Ren's car? Sam the officer instructed to pass. Okay, boys, that's an official order."
[299] Dromio turned onto Waverly Place. The police had set up a cordon here, and traffic had been cut off from Fifth Avenue to Mercer Street. The sidewalk across the street was crowded with onlookers, reporters and photographers buzzed around, and there were police and undercover officers standing by.
[300] The focal point of the storm was immediately apparent, and Dromio pulled the car up to it. It was a three-story, square, red-brick building, an obviously very old-fashioned house from the carriage era – large windows with heavy curtains, a gabled roof with decorative eaves, a row of high white stone steps, with iron railings on either side, connecting to the bottom of the door on both sides, and two rusty iron lionesses standing on the steps. The steps were filled with police officers, the white-paned door was open, and from the sidewalk, one could see a small foyer inside.
[301] Renault, looking somewhat sad, got out of the car. He was wearing a light cotton suit, a straw hat, white shoes, and holding a walking stick. He looked up at the door, sighed, and then stepped up the stone steps. A man stuck his head out of the foyer.
[302] "Mr. Renault?" he said. "Sergeant Sam is waiting for you."
[303] Sergeant Sam – with a face that looked like he had seen too much – greeted Renault. It was a somber interior scene: a long, cool hallway, wide and deep, with a series of closed doors on either side, and an old-fashioned walnut staircase leading up to the second floor. In contrast to the bustling street outside, the interior was eerily quiet – at least as far as Renault's eyes could see, there were no police officers.
[304] "Well," Sergeant Sam said, "it's happened. He seemed to be at a loss for words, "it's happened." As if that was the only thing he could say.
[305] "Is it about Louise Cabian?" Renault asked. The question seemed redundant, as someone had tried to kill her two months ago. Who else could it be?
[306] Inspector Sam answered with regret, "No."
[307] Rayne was startled, almost comically so: "Not Miss Louisa Cabian! Who...?"
[308] "An elderly woman, murdered!"
[309] They stood in the cool hallway, looking at each other, finding no consolation in each other's faces.
[310] "Mrs. Blackthorn," Rayne repeated for the third time. "It's odd, Inspector. It seems someone tried to kill the Blackthorn family, not just one person."
[311] Sergeant Sam impatiently approached the stairs: "You think so?"
[312] "I just think so," Raine said a little awkwardly, "Obviously, you don't agree."
[313] The Sergeant walked heavily, as if carrying a heavy burden: "I don't disagree, I just don't know what to think."
[314] "Poisoned?"
[315] "No, at least it doesn't look like it. You can see for yourself later."
[316] At the top of the stairs, they stopped. Raine's eyes sharpened as they stood in front of a corridor with closed doors, and a police officer stood in front of each door.
[317] "Are these bedrooms, sergeant?"
[318] Sam grunted and stepped over the wooden railing beside the staircase. He suddenly tensed and stopped abruptly, and Raine bumped into him without noticing.
[319] There was a large policeman standing with his back to the door at the northwest corner of the corridor, who let out an "Ah!" and stepped back when the door behind him suddenly opened.
[320] The sergeant sighed in relief: "It's those two damn brats again," he muttered, "Hoke, can't you keep those two little brats in the nursery?"
[321] "Yes, sir," Hoke panted, looking flustered. A small boy came running out from between the sergeant's legs, shouting and determined to make his way down the hallway. Hoke just managed to regain his balance when another, even smaller boy, who looked barely old enough to walk, came charging out from between the sergeant's legs, mimicking the first and shouting as he ran down the hallway. The sergeant chased after them, followed by a worried-looking woman who shrieked, "Jake! Billy! Oh, you boys – you can't do this!"
[322] "Martha Hight?" Raine whispered. She was a fairly attractive woman, but her eyes were lined with worry and exhaustion. Sam nodded, watching the chaos unfold. Hoke was struggling to restrain the thirteen-year-old Jake. From his shouting, it was clear that Billy was trying to see what was happening, and he was kicking the sergeant's legs, causing the sergeant pain and frustration. Martha Hight held onto her youngest son, who was kicking wildly and energetically at the sergeant's knees. In this flurry of punches, kicks, and red faces, the four fighters disappeared into the nursery. The commotion continued, just moving to a different location.
[323] "Well," Sergeant Sam said sarcastically, "that's just a sample of this crazy, creepy family – those two little devils have already made us feel like we're in hell – there, Raine sir."
[324] A door stood directly opposite the staircase, just a few feet from the east-facing wall of the hallway. The door was half-open, and Sam pushed it open solemnly before stepping aside. Raine paused at the threshold, his eyes flickering with caution.
[325] The room was roughly square, a bedroom. There were two protruding windows on the wall opposite the room, overlooking the garden at the back of the house. Near the windows, on the east-facing wall, there was a door, which Sam explained led to a private bathroom. The door where Raine and Sam stood was on the left-hand side of the wall separating the room from the hallway. Raine noticed that on the right was a long, deep closet, which explained why the hallway was narrower at the top of the stairs – the closet took up extra space – and then along the closet, there was another room that continued eastward.
[326] From Raine's vantage point, there were two beds – both single-sized – placed against the right-hand wall, separated by a large bedside table with about two feet of space between the table and each bed. The headboard of the bed closest to the door had a small lamp, while the bed further inside had no lamp. In the center of the left-hand wall, directly opposite the beds, was an old-fashioned stone fireplace, although a set of firebox hung nearby, looking as if it had been abandoned for a long time.
[327] These observations were made intuitively and quickly. After glancing at the general layout of the furniture, Raine's gaze returned to the two beds.
[328] "Dead stiff as last year's codfish," Sam the officer muttered, leaning against the doorpost. "Look at it, isn't it beautiful?" He gestured to the bed.
[329] On the bed closest to the door – the one with the lamp – lay Mrs. Hite. Sam's comment was redundant, as the old lady lay in a rumpled nightgown in a twisted position, with her eyes wide open and unseeing, her face contorted, her veins bulging, and her skin turning purple – the most unlifelike of all living beings. There were several distinct marks on her forehead – several bloody streaks stretching through her tangled, dry hair.
[330] Raine squinted at the bloodstains, showing confusion, and then turned his attention to the other bed. That bed was empty, with only a pile of clean pajamas on it.
[331] "Miss Lulu Cabian's bed?"
[332] Sergeant Sam nodded: "That's where the deaf, dumb, and blind woman slept, but we moved her out of this room earlier this morning when she was found unconscious on the floor over there."
[333] Rine raised his silver eyebrows: "Knocked out?"
[334] "I don't think so. I'll tell you more later. She's in the next room — Miss Smith's bedroom, where the nurse is taking care of her."
[335] "So Miss Cabian is unharmed?"
[336] Sam's face grew serious: "Interesting, isn't it? Based on past events, everyone would assume that whoever is behind this is after her, but she's fine. It's the old lady who got caught."
[337] Footsteps echoed down the hallway behind them, and both turned quickly. Renault's face lit up: "Mr. Bruno! It's a pleasure."
[338] They shook hands warmly. Walter Bruno, the District Attorney of New York County, was a medium-height man with no-framed glasses, muscular, and had a stern-looking face.
[339] He looked tired: "Nice to see you, Mr. Renault. We probably wouldn't meet unless someone met an unfortunate end. Have you been here long?"
[340] "All your fault, just like Chief Sam, you forgot about me all winter. Have you been here long?"
[341] "Half an hour, give or take. What do you think?"
[342] "I don't know," the veteran actor continued to survey the room, "what's going on?"
[343] The prosecutor leaned against the doorpost. "I just saw Miss Kibian. Poor thing. The body was discovered this morning at six by Miss Smith - she sleeps in the room next door and can see the garden and the eastern hallway behind the house…"
[344] "What do you mean, Mr. Bruno?" Rayne murmured.
[345] Bruno shrugged. "Maybe it's significant. Anyway, Lucy usually gets up early, and Miss Smith usually gets up at six to check on her. She found Mrs. Blackthorn just as you see her now, lying in bed, and Lucy collapsed on the floor, roughly in the middle between her bed and the fireplace over there. Come, I'll show you." He was about to step into the bedroom when Rayne placed a hand on his arm.
[346] "I can imagine it," he said, "and I think it would be better if we moved less on the floor. Please continue."
[347] Bruno curiously looked: "Oh, you mean these footprints! Uh, Miss Smith saw the old lady dead, she thought Lucy was dead too, so she screamed, women are women, her scream woke up Barbara and Conrad Heat, they ran in, looked around the scene, didn't touch anything -"
[348] "Are you sure about this?"
[349] "Well, their confessions match, so we have to believe—they touched nothing, they were sure Mrs. Bates was dead, she was indeed stiff, but they found that Louise was only unconscious. They carried her into Miss Smith's room from here, and Conrad called Dr. Mirean, the family doctor, and the police, without letting anyone else in here."
[350] "Milian announced Mrs. Black's death and then went to the nurse's room," Sam added, "to take care of the deaf and dumb one, who wasn't there, and we haven't had a chance to talk to her yet."
[351] Raine nodded thoughtfully: "What exactly was Miss Kappian found like? I want a more precise description, Mr. Bruno?"
[352] "She was found with her limbs spread out, face down. The doctor said she fainted, she had a bump on her forehead, Dr. Mireen's theory is that she hit her forehead on the floor when she fainted, which doesn't help the case much. She's awake now, but still a bit dizzy, it's still a question whether she knows what happened to her mother, Dr. Mireen hasn't allowed us to notify her."
[353] "Has the body been examined?"
[354] "Only a preliminary examination by Dr. Mireen," Bruno said, Sam nodded in agreement, "No formal examination yet, we're waiting for the coroner, Dr. Thuring is notorious for being slow."
[355] Raine sighed. Then he turned resolutely back to the room, looking down. His gaze fell on the green shag carpet that covered the entire room, from where he stood, he could see some white powder-like footprints, widely spaced, they seemed to start from the area between the two single beds, although from Raine's position, he couldn't see. The footprints pointed towards the door leading to the corridor, and were most distinct near the old lady's bedside, becoming more blurred as they approached the door.
[356] Ren follows the trail of footprints, stopping in front of the space between the two beds so he can examine the starting point of the footprints more closely. Now he sees that the footprints are scattered all over a thick layer of white powder on the green carpet between the two beds; the source of the powder is quickly revealed, there is a nearly empty, large, round, white talc powder box on the floor near the foot of Louisa Kabbian's bed - according to the label on the box, it's talcum powder, and there is no part of the carpet between the two beds that doesn't have talcum powder on it.
[357] Ren carefully avoids stepping on the footprints and the powder, sidestepping between the two beds to get a clearer view of the headboards and the floor. Obviously, the talcum powder box was originally placed on the edge of the bedside table, as there are traces of white powder on the table, and there is a circular powder ring on the wooden tabletop that indicates where the powder box was placed before it fell over. A few inches behind the circular powder ring on the wooden desktop, there is a new indentation, as if made by a hard object being struck forcefully.
[358] "In my opinion," Ren concludes, "the box was not properly closed, so it fell open when it hit the ground." He bends down to pick up the powder box lid from the table leg, "You've already observed all this, haven't you?" Sam and Bruno nod wearily.
[359] The top of the white cardboard box lid near the edge has several thin parallel lines, which are red. Ren looks up suspiciously at the two of them.
[360] "It's blood." The chief inspector says.
[361] The part of the box lid where the blood lines are located sinks down, as if the object that caused the blood lines had struck it with great force, flattening even the edge of the lid. Rene nods.
[362] "Without a doubt, gentlemen," he says, "the powder box was struck hard and knocked off the table. There are marks of impact on the desktop and the box lid, and the talc powder is scattered all over the carpet near Miss Cabian's bed."
[363] He places the indented box lid back in its original position and continues to search. There is too much to see.
[364] He decides to check the footprints first. There are several shoe tip prints in the thickest powder between the two beds, each about four inches apart, running parallel from the head of the bed to the foot, in the direction of the fireplace. Almost at the edge of the powder, there are two shoe tip prints clearly visible in the thick talc powder, stepping over the dead man's bed and heading towards the door, the heels and tips of the shoes clearly visible, and the steps getting longer as they go.
[365] "Basically," Rene says quietly, "the person who left these footprints started to run as soon as they walked around the bed."
[366] The footprints resembled running, imprinted on the carpet where no powder had been scattered – caused by the powder on the runner's shoes.
[367] "On the surface, Inspector," Ren looked up, "I would say you are lucky. These are men's footprints."
[368] "We may be lucky, or we may not," Sam grumbled, "I don't like the look of these footprints. They're too clear! Anyway, we have taken measurements from some of the clearer footprints, which are size seven and a half, or eight, or eight and a half shoes, narrow foot, with both heels worn down. My men are currently searching the house for matching shoes."
[369] "Well, it may be quite simple," Ren commented, turning back to the area near the bedtail between the two beds, "So, I guess Miss Cabian was found lying near her bed's foot, at the edge of the powder area, almost at the point where the person's footprints changed direction?"
[370] "Yes, she left some footprints herself, which you can see."
[371] Rine nodded. From the area where talcum powder was scattered to the spot where Louisa Kabbian fell, there were barefoot footprints of a woman, the barefoot footprints began from the corner where the bedsheet was lifted at the deaf woman's bedside, along her bedside until the foot of the bed.
[372] "I guess that should be beyond any doubt, right?"
[373] "No doubt about it," Bruno answered, "They have confirmed that it is her footprints, and it's easy to prove that part, obviously she crawled down the bedside to the foot of the bed, and then something happened that made her faint."
[374] Mr. Philo Rine's eyebrows furrowed, as if something troubled him, he carefully approached Mrs. Blackthorn's bedside and leaned over to examine the dead woman. He spent some time observing the peculiar mark on her forehead that he had noticed earlier, consisting of several fine, long, parallel lines, of varying lengths, and slightly inclined to one side - towards the direction of the bedside table. These lines did not cross the entire forehead; they began between the eyebrows and the hairline, and then extended into the stiff white hair.
[375] Blood was oozing from these strange lines. As if to confirm it, Rine's gaze flowed to the carpet under the bedside table, and he nodded. There, half-hidden under the table, on the front of the chair, lay a broken old mandolin.
[376] He bends down to examine it more closely - then turns to look at his two companions, Prosecutor Bruno with a sarcastic smile. "You found it," he says. "The murder weapon."
[377] "Yes," Renet replies in a low voice. "You can see that there is blood on the lower part of the steel string. One of the strings is broken, and all of them are rusted, as if no one had played them for a long time, but the blood stains are unmistakable."
[378] Renet picks up the mandolin lying in the powder, observing it as he does so. The impression of the instrument on the powder is clear, and he also notices a very new dent on the bottom edge of the instrument, which appears to match the dent in the table.
[379] "What do you think, Mr. Renet, an extraordinary murder weapon," Inspector Sam says with a disgruntled tone. "Killing someone with a mandolin! I can't believe it! Next time they'll use a flower."
[380] "Very strange, very strange," Renet says with a blank expression. "So, Mrs. Blackthorn was struck on the forehead with a mandolin - an unusual choice for a murder weapon. The odd thing about this case, gentlemen, is not the choice of weapon, but the fact that the weapon did not have enough force to kill. Based on the depth of the impact marks, it should not have been fatal. Yes, it is indeed very strange... We could use Dr. Thuring's expertise here."
[381] He placed the mandolin back on the carpet in the same spot it had been in, then turned his attention to the bedside table. There was nothing out of place: a dish of fruit (closer to the bed of the deaf, dumb, and blind lady), a clock, the residue of a spilled powder puff, a Bible with a pair of heavy bookends holding it open, and a wilted bouquet of flowers.
[382] The fruit dish contained an apple, a banana, a bunch of early grapes, an orange, and three pears.
[383] Dr. Leo Thayer, the Chief Medical Examiner of New York County, was not a man of many emotions. His long career handling all manner of bizarre corpses – suicides, murder victims, unidentified bodies, cadavers from the lab, drug addicts, and countless others who had died in strange circumstances, of natural causes, or from violence – had made him a hardened man. He scorned the idea of being "fastidious," and his nerve and skill with a scalpel were as unshakeable as his colleagues suspected his heart might be, though no one had ever been able to confirm it.
[384] He strode into the late Mrs. Blackett's boudoir, barely acknowledging the prosecutor with a nod, grunting at Sam, muttering incoherently to Leroy, taking in the room with a single glance, and then, with a somber expression, noting the footprints on the carpet. He then tossed his briefcase onto the bed – Leroy was startled by the sound of it hitting the stiff leg of the old woman.
[385] "Is it all right to step on the footprints?" Dr. Thayer asked abruptly.
[386] "Yes," the detective said, "everything has been photographed as evidence. And I have to tell you, doctor, next time you should try to be a little more punctual. I've been trying to reach you for over two and a half hours -"
[387] "Es besteht immer noch eine alte Geschichte, aber bleibt immer neu," the short doctor said in German, smiling, "as Heinrich Heine put it, though my translation doesn't do it justice: although it's an old story, it's always new... Take it easy, detective, this deceased lady was quite patient."
[388] He pushed the brim of his cap up - he was quite bald and sensitive about it - and walked around the bed with a lack of care, stomping indifferently on the footprints and getting to work.
[389] The smile vanished from his round face, and his eyes behind the old-fashioned gold-rimmed glasses became very focused. Mr. Jury Lean noticed that when he saw the vertical bloodstain on the deceased's forehead, his purple lips puckered, and he nodded when he saw the mandolin on the floor. He then carefully lifted the deceased's head between his two strong hands and began to comb through her hair, touching various parts of her skull quickly.
[390] Obviously something was amiss, as his face grew rigid, and he pulled back the tangled sheets, spending a minute checking the body.
[391] They stood in silence. It was clear that the experienced forensic doctor was becoming increasingly puzzled; he muttered in German, "Damn it!" several times, shaking his head, pursing his lips, and occasionally humming a drinking song... Suddenly, he turned to face everyone.
[392] "Where is the deceased's personal doctor?"
[393] Inspector Sam left the room and returned two minutes later with Dr. Mirean. The two doctors greeted each other formally, like duelists, and Dr. Mirean walked around the bed with dignity. They both bent over the body, pulling the thin sleeping robe up, checking the body while whispering to each other. At that moment, Nurse Smith, who was plump, rushed into the room, snatched the fruit tray from the bedside table, and quickly left.
[394] Sam, Bruno, and Rene watched silently.
[395] Finally, the doctors stood up, and Dr. Mirean's face showed some concern, while the forensic doctor pulled his cap low over his forehead, which was covered in sweat.
[396] "What's your verdict, doctor?"
[397] Dr. Schelling looked grave. "The woman did not die from a blow." - Mr. Philo Vance nodded in satisfaction - "Dr. Miller and I agree that the blow itself, while startling, did not cause further harm."
[398] "So, what killed her?" Detective Sam growled.
[399] "Well, detective, if you insist on getting to the point," Dr. Schelling said with some irritation, "what's the rush? It was the mandolin that killed her, indirectly. Oh? What was that? The blow caused her to become severely frightened, why? Because she was old - sixty-three - and Dr. Miller says she had serious heart disease. Isn't that right, doctor?"
[400] "Oh," Detective Sam replied, looking somewhat relieved, "I see. Someone hit her on the head, and that blow frightened her weak heart, so she died. So, she probably died in her sleep, then."
[401] "I don't think so," Mr. Vance said. "On the contrary, Inspector, she was not only not sleeping, but she was extremely and very much awake." - Both doctors nodded in agreement - "There are three reasons. First, notice her eyes are open, wide and staring, frightened, she was awake, Inspector… Second, you can see the unique expression on her face," - the harsh words were so gentle that Emily Hatch's aged features twisted in extreme pain and sudden terror - "even her hands are half clenched, her fingers spread… Third, this is more subtle," Vance walked to the bedside and pointed to the bloodstains on the dead woman's forehead caused by the mandolin strings. "These bloodstains unquestionably prove that Mrs. Blackthorne was sitting on the bed when she was attacked."
[402] "How do you know?" Inspector Sam asked, somewhat annoyed.
[403] "Why, it's simple. If she had been hit while sleeping - in other words, lying down, and from her general posture, it seems she was lying flat on her back - then the steel string's wounds would not only have appeared on the top of her forehead, but also on the lower part, and perhaps even on her nose and lips. Since the bloodstains are limited to the top, it can be seen that she was either sitting or in a semi-sitting position. If that's the case, we can immediately conclude that she was awake."
[404] "That's a brilliant observation, sir," Dr. Miller said, standing stiffly and nervously twisting his long fingers.
[405] "It's just a very basic observation. Dr. Sherington, what time do you estimate Mrs. Blackthorne died?"
[406] Dr. Thornhill took a toothpick from his vest pocket and began to poke at his teeth: "She's been dead for six hours, which means she died around four o'clock this morning."
[407] Rinehart nodded: "There is one point that may be important, doctor, the exact location of the killer when he attacked Mrs. Heat. Can you elaborate on that?"
[408] Dr. Thornhill narrowed his eyes thoughtfully at the bed: "I think so, the killer was standing between the two beds — not on the outside of the old lady's bed, I deduce this from the position of the body and the bloodstains on her forehead. What do you think, Dr. Merrill?"
[409] The old doctor was startled: "Ah — I completely agree," he hastily answered.
[410] Sergeant Sam scratched his thick chin irritably: "Mandolin, this whole thing — somehow, doesn't feel right to me. I mean — no matter how good or bad the heart is, hitting someone with a mandolin string like that couldn't possibly kill them, could it? I mean — if someone really wanted to kill someone, even if they chose a strange weapon, they would choose one that could kill, wouldn't they?"
[411] "Well, it's not entirely impossible, Sam," the medical examiner replies. "While a mandolin is not a particularly lethal weapon, it could potentially kill someone like Mrs. Blackthorn, who was in poor health and elderly. However, in this case, the blow appears to be quite weak."
[412] "Are there any other signs of violence on the body?" Rine asks.
[413] "No."
[414] "Poison?" the prosecutor inquires. "Any indications?"
[415] "No indications," Dr. Schelling answers carefully. "However, on the other hand—yes, I should perform an autopsy immediately."
[416] "You can bet your German boots it's a must, Sam," the detective took the opportunity to get back at him, "Make sure there's no more poison around. I don't understand this case, first someone tries to poison the deaf one, now someone bludgeons the old hag to death. I'll be looking around for any signs of poison."
[417] Bruno's sharp eyes gleamed, "This is certainly a murder, even if the blow itself wasn't the direct cause of death - it was the shock. One thing is certain: someone had the intent to kill."
[418] "So why hit so lightly, Mr. Bruno?" Rine asked without emotion, the prosecutor shrugged, "And why, may I ask, choose such an unusual weapon? - A mandolin! If the killer's intention was to bludgeon Mrs. Black to death from above, why choose a mandolin when there are several heavy weapons in this room?"
[419] "My God, I didn't think of that," Sam murmured as Rine pointed out the fireplace tongs hanging by the fireplace and the heavy bookends on the bedside table.
[420] Rine turned to glance around the room, his hands lightly clasped behind his back, Dr. Shering began to show signs of impatience, Dr. Miller stood rigidly like a soldier awaiting inspection, and the district attorney and Sam looked increasingly confused.

[422] "No," the inspector replied, "it was brought from the glass cabinet in the library downstairs. After York Blackthorn committed suicide, the old lady kept it there – it was one of her widow's treasures, and it belonged to York… Hey, speaking of which –"

[424] Raine stepped forward and picked it up.
[425] It was a hypodermic syringe.
[426] They all gathered around, their spirits lifted by this important discovery. Ren carefully held the syringe by the barrel, sniffed the needle that had been used to inject the drug, and then held it up to the light.

[432] "No."

[434] Everyone followed.
[435] Miss Smith's room was adjacent to the deceased's room. As they entered, they were presented with a serene scene.
[436] There, lying on the bed with blind eyes, was a fat body that was relaxed and comfortable. A plump old nurse was sitting on the bedside chair, stroking the deaf woman's forehead. Louisa mechanically picked grapes from a string and stuffed them into her mouth, chewing listlessly. On the table near the bed was a fruit tray that Miss Smith had brought from the deceased's bedroom just moments ago.
[437] Without a word, Jules Raine rushed into the room, snatching the grapes from Louisa's hand. His action was so forceful that Miss Smith jumped up from the chair in shock, and the deaf and blind woman sat up on the bed, her lips moving as she looked frightened. The usually expressionless face showed signs of panic, and she began to whimper like a startled animal, reaching out to grab Miss Smith's hand and clutching it tightly. Her trembling skin came alive, and goosebumps appeared all over her arms.
[438] "How much did she eat?" Raine blurted out.

[440] Dr. Merrill quickly rushed to the bedside. The woman stopped whimpering as soon as she felt him touch her forehead.

[445] "Yes," Vance replied, his deep voice resonating. "And gentlemen, the situation of the case has… changed."

[449] "Does Miss Cabot have a particular preference for any fruit?"

[459] Mr. Philo Raine seemed quite satisfied, nodding several times and giving Miss Smith a friendly glance. Then, looking down at the fruit tray from the table next to the nurse's bed, he said, "She doesn't like pears. Notice, Inspector, I dare say these pears should be carefully examined."
[460] "She doesn't like pears," he murmured. "Look, Inspector, I dare say these pears should be carefully examined."

[462] Mr. Vance sighed and held the pear up to his right eye about three inches away. "As I suspected," he said, turning triumphantly to Dr. Sherringford, "here, doctor, take these three pears. You will find a needle hole in the rotten one, unless I am mistaken."

[469] "Indeed, now we have a lead. The poisoned pear was undoubtedly the work of the same person who poisoned the eggnog two months ago."

[471] Act III
[472] Library, June 5, Sunday, 11:10 a.m.

It was a flat board with grooves, somewhat like an abacus, and a large box. She opened the lid, and inside were many small metal blocks, like domino game cards, each with a protruding part at the back that could be inserted into the grooves of the board. The surface of the blocks had large and raised dots arranged in special and various combinations on the blocks.
[481] "Braille?" Rayne asked.
[482] "Yes," Miss Smith sighed, "Each block represents a letter of Braille, and this board is specially designed for Louisa… She takes it with her wherever she goes."
[483] To assist non-experts in reading this blind person's "writing" language, each surface of the blocks also had a flat white English letter painted on it - the translation of the Braille letter represented by that block.
[484] "Very clever," Rayne commented, "If you don't mind, Miss Smith…" He gently pushed the nurse aside and picked up the board and blocks, looking down at Louisa Cabian.
[485] Everyone felt that this was a critical moment. How would this tragic, extraordinary woman react? It was clear that she had already sensed the unusual tension around her. Her white, beautiful fingers kept twitching - not long ago she had withdrawn her hand from Dr. Mirian's - and Rayne noticed with a slight shudder that those twitching fingers resembled insect antennae, moving with a wise urgency seeking answers. Her head jerked anxiously and jerkily from side to side, further reinforcing the insect-like association. Her pupils were large but vacant - the eyes of a blind person. At this moment, with all eyes on her, no one noticed that, in terms of appearance, she was no different from a normal person, perhaps even somewhat likable - she was quite plump, standing at most five feet four inches tall, with thick brown hair and a healthy complexion.
[486] But what attracted everyone's attention was her strange expression - fish-like eyes and a still, vacant, almost lifeless face, along with the twitching fingers...

[492] In the silence, Mr. Leen sighed and took the woman's hands in his strong ones. They struggled at first, then relaxed as she sniffed at him, an odd sensation that seemed to calm her, or perhaps she sensed a subtle scent that most humans couldn't perceive, and her features softened. Her fingers slipped from Leen's palm...
[493] What happened. Where's Mom. Who are you.
[494] Leen quickly picked out some blocks from the box and arranged them into a series of sentences. He placed the board on Louisa's legs, and she eagerly grabbed it, her fingers moving over the metal blocks.
[495] "I'm a friend," Leen's message read. "I'm here to help. I have some bad news for you. You must be brave."

[497] Miss Smith frowned and translated.
[498] Yes. Yes. I am brave. What happened.
[499] Renet's fingers delved into the box, rearranging the letters to form new sentences... The room was so quiet that you could hear a pin drop.
[500] "Your life is an epic of a hero. Keep going. A great tragedy has occurred. Your mother was killed last night."

She had a radiance within that seemed to glow from within and illuminate her appearance, making her actions warm and inviting. The poetess Barbara Haight was not only a wise woman, but also an unusual person with a delicate feeling.

[1374] Barbara Blackthorn rushed up the hallway: "Oh, my God!... There's no fire extinguisher, sergeant... Martha – the child!"
[1375] The corridor was shrouded in mist, and people were rushing about in a panic. Flames began to lick out from under the laboratory door. Dressed in a silk robe, Martha screamed and ran towards the nursery, soon emerging with two boys in tow. Billy shrieked in terror, and even Jock, who rarely got scared, clung tightly to his mother's hand. They all rushed down the stairs and disappeared from sight.
[1376] "Everyone out! Out!" Sam shouted loudly, "Don't stop to grab things! Those chemicals — they'll explode —" His roar was drowned out by screams. Ginger Hight stumbled past him, her face pale and terrified; Conrad Hight pushed her aside and fled down the stairs in a panic; and Edgar Perrin, dressed in his pajamas, emerged from the attic and collided with Barbara Hight, who was coughing and stumbling to the ground. He slung her over his shoulder and carried her down the stairs.
[1377] Everyone was coughing and wheezing, with tear-filled eyes.
[1378] The detectives on the roof ran down, pushing Mr. and Mrs. Abigail and Vicky ahead of them. The sergeant was dazed, coughing, choking, and shouting as he lugged bucket after bucket of water onto the closed laboratory door. He heard the fire truck's siren...
[1379] The situation was dire. The screeching of brakes announced the arrival of the fire truck, and firefighters sprang into action, dragging hoses along the garden path to the rear of the house. Flames licked out from behind the iron railings of the windows, and the fire ladder was raised. The firefighters smashed the unbroken window glass with axes, and streams of water shot into the laboratory through the iron bars...
[1380] As the firefighters struggled to drag the hoses inside and up the stairs, disheveled, soot-covered, and bloodshot-eyed Sam stood on the sidewalk outside, counting the shivering, thinly-clad heads around him. Everyone was there... not everyone was there!
[1381] The chief of police's face twisted in pain and terror as he ran up the stairs, barging into the house, rushing upstairs, stumbling over the wet hoses. Once upstairs, he dashed to Miss Smith's room, followed closely by Mueller.
[1382] He kicked open the door, barging into the nurse's room. Miss Smith lay on the ground, unconscious, in a large, white robe; Louise Kabiyan looked like a wild animal trapped, bewildered, shivering, and crawling over the nurse, sniffing the acrid smoke.
[1383] Sam and Mueller struggled to get the two women out of the house...
[1384] And just in time. As they stumbled down the stone steps outside, a dull boom sounded behind them, from above their heads - a burst of fire exploded from the laboratory at the back of the house. A deafening explosion was followed by a moment of stunned silence, then the firefighters' screams from within the inferno...
[1385] An inevitable event occurred - some chemicals in the laboratory exploded due to the fire.
[1386] An ambulance arrived, carrying stretcher after stretcher in and out. One firefighter was injured.
[1387] Two hours later, the fire was extinguished, and the last fire truck left as dawn broke. The Blackthorn family and others who had sought shelter in Captain Quiver's brick house next door, tiredly returned to the charred mansion. Captain Quiver, in his pajamas and robe, made hollow sounds on the pavement with his wooden leg as he assisted the awakening Miss Smith in caring for the hysterical Luceysha Cabian. Dr. Merrill arrived on the scene and administered sedatives.
[1388] The laboratory upstairs was a sight to behold. The door had been blown off, the iron bars of the windows were loose, most of the bottles and jars on the shelves were shattered, and the floor was wet. The bed, wardrobe, and desk were all charred, and most of the distillers, test tubes, and electronic instruments had their glass melted. Interestingly, the damage to the rest of the second floor was minimal.
[1389] Sam, with dark circles under his eyes, gathered everyone in the ground-floor library and sitting room, where policemen were stationed in various places. No one dared to joke or defy orders, and most of the time, they sat quietly, with women even more subdued than men, staring blankly at each other.
[1390] The officer picked up the phone and dialed the police headquarters. He first spoke with Prosecutor Bruno and then had a long, somber conversation with Chief of Police Barbergh. He then dialed a long-distance number to Hamlet Manor in Blaskeer, New York.
[1391] There was a problem with the line. Sam waited patiently, a rarity for him. When he finally heard the gruff, trembling voice of Quincannon, his hunchbacked servant, he recounted the events of the evening in detail. J. Rhein, who was deaf and could not answer the phone himself, stood next to Quincannon, learning the story through Quincannon's lip-reading.
[1392] "Mr. Rhein asks if you know how the fire started," Sam said after he finished speaking.
[1393] "Tell him that the chimney entrance on the roof was watched every second, the windows were locked from the inside, and there was no tampering with the laboratory door all night," Sam said. "My man, Moddy, was watching it."
[1394] The sergeant heard Quincannon repeat these words and then heard Rhein's deep voice on the other end of the line.
[1395] "He asks if you're sure, Sergeant?"
[1396] "My God, of course I'm sure! That's why I don't understand how the firefly got in to start the fire?"
[1397] Quincannon repeats this, and there is silence. The chief waits, ears perked, then Quincannon says, "Rine wants to know if anyone tried to enter the lab after the explosion?"
[1398] "No," Sam roars, "I kept a close eye on that."
[1399] "He says to send someone to guard the room immediately," Quincannon says sharply, "unless there are more firefighters coming. Rine will be there today, and he's certain he knows what happened, he says…"
[1400] "Oh, he's certain, is he?" the chief asks impatiently, "Well, he's better than me. Hey! Ask him if he expected the fire!"
[1401] There was a pause, and then Quincannon answered, "No, he said he didn't expect it, it was a complete surprise to him, he couldn't understand."
[1402] "Thank God, there's something that can stump him," Sam roared. "Well – tell him to come early."
[1403] As he was about to hang up, he distinctly heard Raine whisper to Quincannon, "It must be – everything points to this – but, Quincannon, it's too incredible!"

[1432] Mr. Philo Rinehart sighed, relieved that the mystery was solved without soiling his clothes.
[1433] The inspector became increasingly enthusiastic about this lead, his entire demeanor changing. He slapped Rinehart on the back, his frog-like face beaming with pleasure, and barked orders to his men, kicking Pixton off his chair and handing a cigar to the fireman.

[1435] The secret of the fireplace was quite simple. The laboratory's fireplace and Miss Louisa Cabbell's room were connected—the fireplaces were on opposite sides of the same wall, sharing not only a chimney but also a six-foot-high, thick firebrick wall that was only four feet high from the floor on both sides, making the top of the wall invisible from either room. From the top of the six-foot-high partition, the two flues merged into one large chimney, through which the smoke from both fireplaces was expelled to the roof.
[1436] "Quite clear, indeed, quite clear," the inspector exclaimed excitedly. "This means that anyone could have entered the laboratory at any time—either by climbing over the partition wall from the interior of the deceased's room or by descending from the roof via the hand and foot nails in the chimney. Last night, someone must have entered through Louisa's room, which is why Ned never saw anyone entering the laboratory from the hallway, and the guards on the roof didn't see anyone either!"
[1437] "Indeed," Renault said. "And, of course, your visitor also escaped through the same route. Have you considered, Inspector, that the first thing our mysterious arsonist had to do was to get into Miss Cabian's room, where Ned had been watching the door all night, you know."

Two long and narrow fire escapes were located outside on the second floor, one on the side of the laboratory and nursery, and the other on the side of the deceased's room and Miss Smith's room. Both fire escapes led to the attic windows and then down to the garden floor.

[1452] "Of course," Raine joked. "Our analysis could be completely wrong, you know. Maybe the fire was spontaneous."

No, someone deliberately took it from the shelf and placed it on the table, knowing the result would be an explosion."

[1499] Second scene
[1500] Garden June 6, Monday, 4:00 pm
[1501] If Sam Sergeant had been a psychologist, or if he hadn't had so many other things on his mind, the eccentric Blackthorn family could have provided him with an interesting study opportunity that day. Being confined to the house, they roamed aimlessly, picking things up and putting them down, shooting hateful glances at each other, and avoiding each other as much as possible. Gertrude and Conrad argued constantly, bickering over the smallest things, and even the slightest provocation caused a conflict. They spewed hurtful words at each other, and even the most impatient person would have difficulty denying their fault. Martha kept her children close, occasionally hitting and scolding them, but only when Conrad Blackthorn passed by did she snap out of her numbness, glaring at him with a bitter look, even though the children noticed and couldn't help but ask why.
[1502] The more Sam thought about the confusing clues, the more agitated he became. The thought of Philo Rine possibly having a lead on the case and wondering what answer he might have made him uneasy. However, Rine seemed troubled for some special reason, and Sam couldn't figure out what it was. Twice during the afternoon, he walked over to the phone to call Hamlet Manor, but each time he placed his hand on the receiver, only to realize he had nothing to ask or say.
[1503] The strange chimney passage began to pique Sam's imagination, and he left Rine behind, going upstairs to the laboratory to measure the firebrick wall separating the two fireplaces. To his satisfaction, he discovered that an adult man could easily climb from one room to another through the chimney without much effort... yes, even his large shoulders could rotate freely in the space.
[1504] He climbed back down to the laboratory and called Pixon to gather the family.
[1505] They came in one by one, uninterested in the latest review, and the shock of all the bizarre events and the fire had made them numb to any surprises. When everyone was present, the sergeant began a series of seemingly unexpected ordinary questions. They answered mechanically, at least as far as Sam knew, and they were all apparently honest. When asked about the chimney passage, he carefully did not reveal the secret passage, believing that either the culprit was an excellent actor or that everyone was telling the truth. He had hoped to coax someone into confessing or to dig up a lie from someone's forgotten memories, but by the end of the questioning, the sergeant had no more information than he had before.
[1506] When he ordered them to disperse, the group filed out one by one, and Officer Sam collapsed into a chair in the library, pondering where he had gone wrong.
[1507] "Officer."
[1508] He looked up to find Mr. Pire standing in front of him.
[1509] "Hmm, what do you want, sir?" Sam growled.
[1510] Mr. Pire quickly replied, "I request permission to take a day off. I... these events have made me... uh, officer, yesterday was usually my day off, but since we're not allowed to leave the house, and I feel like I need some fresh air...".
[1511] Sam cut him off. Pere nervously shifted his feet, but a glimmer of anticipation shone in his eyes, and Sam, instead of the sharp words on his lips, spoke in a kind tone, "I'm sorry, Pere, but it's just not possible. Unless we get to the bottom of this, everyone has to stay in the house."
[1512] The glow faded, and Pere slumped, leaving the library without a word, walking down the hallway to the back of the house, and then into the garden. Clouds loomed overhead, and he hesitated before seeing Barbara Hitch sitting quietly under a garden umbrella, reading a book. He bounded across the lawn…
[1513] The afternoon dragged on, and the Inspector thought, this case was really dragging. First, there was a whirlwind of events, a dramatic turn of events, an explosive incident—and then nothing, completely nothing, completely devoid of action. There was an unnatural feel to the whole thing, leaving one feeling helpless, and as if the crime was inevitable, as if everything had been planned long ago, and was now inexorably pushing towards an unknown climax. But—what? What would be the outcome?
[1514] During this time, Captain Cutwitch had visited, as was his usual quiet manner, making his way up to the deaf, mute, and blind woman resting in Miss Smith's room, still isolated from the world.
[1515] One of the servants came in to report that Mr. Bigger had arrived to visit Miss Hitch. Greer had not reappeared.
[1516] At four o'clock, as Sam sat idly in the library, one of his most trusted subordinates entered with a sense of urgency, and the inspector immediately perked up.
[1517] They whispered briefly, and Sam's eyes grew brighter with each word.
[1518] Finally, he leapt to his feet, ordering the detective to stand guard at the foot of the stairs while he ran up two flights to the attic.
[1519] He was familiar with the surroundings. The two doors at the back of the house overlooked the garden — one was the bedroom of the maid, Vicky, and the other belonged to Edgar Poirot. The room at the northeast corner was empty, connected to a bathroom between it and the storage room at the southeast corner. To the south was a large storage room with an adjoining bathroom — now used as a storage room, but in the heyday of Blacket House, it was a guest room. The entire western side of the attic was occupied by the Abigail couple.
[1520] The inspector did not hesitate. He strode down the hallway, trying the door handle of Edgar Poirot's bedroom. It was unlocked, and he slipped inside, closing the door behind him. He rushed to a window overlooking the garden, where Poirot sat under the umbrella, engaged in a lively conversation with Barbara.
[1521] The inspector smiled contentedly and got to work.
[1522] The room was neat and tidy – almost as neat and tidy as its occupant. There was a high bed, a wardrobe, a rug, a chair, and a full bookshelf. Everything seemed to be in its proper place.
[1523] Inspector Sam was meticulous and methodical in his search. He seemed particularly interested in the contents of Poirot's wardrobe, but his efforts proved fruitless. He then turned his attention to a small closet, rummaging through every pocket of each garment… He lifted the rug, flipped through all the pages of the books, searched the spaces behind the bookshelves, and lifted the cushions of the bed.
[1524] This thorough, expert search yielded nothing.
[1525] He carefully put everything back in its place and walked over to the window. Poirot was still chatting animatedly with Barbara, while the young Gerald Blackwood sat lazily under a tree, casting seductive glances at Chester Bigglesworth.
[1526] The sergeant descended the stairs.
[1527] He made his way to the rear of the house, descending the wooden steps leading to the garden. A rumble of thunder echoed in the distance, and raindrops began to fall on the umbrella, but neither Pere nor Barbara seemed to notice. However, the sudden interruption caused by Sergeant Sam's appearance caused Biggles and Hight to use the rain as an excuse to quickly get up and go inside. Biggles nodded nervously to the sergeant as he passed, while Hight glared at him.
[1528] Sergeant Sam clasped his hands behind his back and smiled faintly at the gloomy sky before slowly walking across the lawn towards the umbrella.
[1529] Barbara was speaking in a low voice, "But, my dear Mr. Pere, surely..."
[1530] "I insist that there should be no metaphysics in poetry," Pere exclaimed, tapping the spine of a slim book on the table between them, which bore the title "Mysterious Concerto" by Barbara Hight, "Oh, I admit you write beautifully – with poetic grace and rich imagination –"
[1531] She laughs: "Elegance? Oh, thank you! It's nice to discuss something with someone who's not just buttering you up."
[1532] "Ah!" he blushes, looking like a schoolboy and seemingly at a loss for words. Neither of them notices Sam the officer standing in the rain, observing them thoughtfully.
[1533] "Now, take your poem, 'Tar Sands,' for instance, the third stanza begins: 'Mountains painted like a fresco hang—'"
[1534] "Ah," Sam the officer says, "I'm sorry."
[1535] They turn, startled, as Perry's focused expression fades, and he stands up awkwardly, still pressing the book on Barbara's side.
[1536] "I think," said Perry abruptly, "I should go in."
[1537] "Not so fast, Perry," said the cop with a grin, sighing and sitting down in a gentlemanly manner. "Actually, I wanted to talk to you."
[1538] "Oh!" said Barbara. "Then I think I should go in."
[1539] "No, no," said the cop magnanimously, "it's no big deal, just a little matter of formality. Sit down, Perry, sit down. Rainy day, isn't it?"
[1540] The spirit of poetry that had briefly animated this face withdrew its wings and slipped away quietly. Perry looked suddenly much older, and Barbara deliberately avoided looking at his face. A dark, damp feeling seemed to have crept into the umbrella.
[1541] "Well, about your former employer," the sergeant continued in the same affable tone.
[1542] Pere stiffened: "Is?" he asked in a harsh tone.
[1543] "How well do you know James Ridget, who wrote your recommendation letter?"
[1544] His face flushed: "How well…" The tutor stammered, "how can—what—in such a situation."
[1545] "Ah, I see," Sam smiled, "of course. I asked a silly question. How long did you work for him, teaching his children?"
[1546] Perry sat there motionless, looking like an inexperienced knight, and spoke in a voice devoid of emotion: "So you found out."
[1547] "Yes, sir, we did find out," Sam answered, still smiling. "You see, Perry, there's no point in hiding from the police. It's child's play to find out that James Ridgeley doesn't live at the address on your recommendation letter and that there's no James Ridgeley at all. Honestly, it saddens me that you thought you could deceive me with such a lie."
[1548] "Oh, for heaven's sake, stop it!" Perry exclaimed. "What do you want—to arrest me? Just do it, don't torture me like this!"
[1549] The officer's smile vanished, and he sat up straight: "Come on, Perry, tell me the truth."
[1550] Barbara Hite didn't even blink, still staring at the cover of her book.
[1551] "All right," the tutor replied wearily. "I know I'm stupid, and it's bad luck to encounter a murder case while pretending to be employed. Yes, the letter of introduction was a forgery, Inspector."
[1552] "We forged it," Barbara Harte said sympathetically.
[1553] Pirey jumped up, incredulous, and the inspector narrowed his eyes. "What do you mean by that, Miss Harte? That's a very serious accusation in the current circumstances."
[1554] "What I mean," Barbara answered in her deep, clear voice, "is what I said. I knew Pirey before he came here, and he desperately needed a job and didn't want to accept financial assistance. I know my brother Conrad, and since he didn't have a letter of introduction, I convinced him to forge one himself, and it was my fault."
[1555] "Ah," the inspector said, nodding his head like the White Rabbit in Alice's Adventures in Wonderland. "I see, I see, very good, Miss Harte, and very fortunate for you, Pirey, to have such a loyal friend." Pirey's face was as pale as Barbara's dress, and he tugged at the collar of his coat. "So, you couldn't find anyone to write you a letter of introduction?"
[1556] The tutor cleared his throat. "I... I don't know any 'big' people. I really need this job, Detective... the salary is quite generous, and there's an opportunity to be close to Miss Black, uh... Black... Miss Black, her poetry always inspires me... I... this trick worked, and that's how it happened."
[1557] Sam's gaze flicked from Perry to Barbara and back again, while Barbara remained motionless, and Perry looked extremely uncomfortable.
[1558] "Alright, Perry – do you have any references at all? Anyone who can vouch for you?"
[1559] Barbara suddenly stood up. "Isn't my recommendation enough, Detective Sam? Her tone was firm, and her green eyes were stern.
[1560] "Of course, of course, Miss Hight. But I have my duties. What about it?"
[1561] Perry flipped through his book. "To be honest,” he began slowly, "I've never been a tutor before, so I don't have any professional references for you."
[1562] "Ah,” said the inspector, "interesting. So, any references at all - I mean, besides Miss Blackthorn?"
[1563] "I... no one,” Perry stammered, "I have no friends."
[1564] "My goodness,” Sam grinned, "you're an odd one, Perry. Imagine, living your whole life and not finding two people to vouch for you! It reminds me of a story about a guy who lived in America for five years before applying for citizenship. When he found out he needed two citizens to vouch for him, he told the judge he couldn't find two familiar American citizens to testify. Ha! Ha! The judge refused his application - said if he had lived in the country for five years..."
[1565] "I believe,” said Barbara Blackthorn coldly, "you're getting a bit too nosy, Inspector Sam. Mr. Perry hasn't committed any crime. If he has, you should say so. Mr. Perry, you - don't answer. I forbid it. I find this too much!"
[1566] She emerges from under the umbrella, placing her hand on the tutor's arm, and leads him across the lawn back to the house without looking back, both of them seeming like they're in a dream.
[1567] The sergeant sits alone in the rain for a while, smoking a cigarette. He stares at the door where the poet and Perey disappeared, their faces showing a hint of a malicious smile.
[1568] He stands up, slowly crosses the lawn, and enters the house, yelling harshly at a detective.
[1569] Scene Three
[1570] Library, June 7, Tuesday, 1:00 PM
[1571] June 7, Tuesday was a big day for the New York news industry. There were two events worth reporting - first, the funeral of Emily Black, and second, the reading of her will.
[1572] Blacket's body was taken out of the morgue and sent to a funeral parlor for makeup before being quickly transported to the final resting place. All of this happened between Monday evening and Tuesday morning, and by 10:30 a.m. on Tuesday, the funeral procession was already on its way to the Long Island cemetery. The Blacket family seemed unfazed by the solemnity of the funeral, their unusual views on life and death preventing them from shedding tears or displaying the usual signs of mourning. Except for Barbara, they were suspicious of each other and argued all the way to Long Island. The two children refused to stay at home, treating the outing as if it were a picnic in the countryside. They were scolded by their mother throughout the journey, and by the time the group arrived at the cemetery, Martha Blacket was hot, tired, and irritated.

[1576] This was an unusual funeral. The poet's old and new acquaintances from the intellectual circles were all present, not to mourn the deceased but to pay their respects to the living. There were many well-known men and women gathered around the grave.
[1577] On the other hand, the guests belonging to Jeeves Hatch were a group of men, both young and old, all dressed appropriately. They were more concerned with attracting Jeeves' attention and getting close to her than with the funeral itself.

[1584] Biggrow's opening speech is straightforward: "I don't want anyone to interrupt, please, this will is somewhat special in certain aspects, and I don't want any comments before I finish reading it." - there is silence below - "Before I read the will, let me explain that all the inheritance distribution is based on an approximate asset amount of about one million dollars after the removal of legal fees. [1585] He takes a long document from the assistant lawyer's hand, shrugs his shoulders, and then reads out loud in a solemn and dignified manner the last will and testament of Emily Heathe.
[1586] From the first sentence, the will cast an ominous shadow.
[1587] After confirming that she was of sound mind when she made the will, Mrs. Blackthorn coldly explained the main purpose of all the clauses, which was to ensure that her daughter, Lucy Cabian, would be properly cared for after her death, assuming Lucy was still alive at the time of the reading of the will.
[1588] As the eldest child of Emily Blackthorn and York Blackthorn, Barbara Blackthorn was the first choice for the responsibility of caring for an orphaned woman. If Barbara agreed to accept this responsibility and was willing to care for Lucy's physical, psychological, and moral health during her natural life, the inheritance would be distributed as follows:
[1589] Lucy (placed under Barbara's care) ... $300,000 Barbara (her own inheritance) ... $300,000 Conrad ... ... $300,000 Gerald ... ... $100,000
[1590] Under this arrangement, Barbara has the power of attorney over Lucy's inherited property. If Lucy dies, this trust inheritance will be divided equally among the three Blackthorn children, each receiving $100,000. Lucy's death will not affect the original distribution of Barbara, Conrad, or Gerald's inheritance.
[1591]Bickero stopped to catch his breath, and Gigi, with a face twisted with anger, shrieked, "Good! Why did she give…"

[1600] Barbara (due to refusal) — $50,000 Conrad (due to refusal) — $50,000 Gigi (as before) — $100,000 Lucy Kappian Deaf-Blind Home (as before) — $250,000 Lucy — $500,000

[1652] Fourth scene
[1653] Hamlet Manor, June 8, Tuesday, 3:00 PM
[1654] It was a clear Tuesday, but cold. The countryside along the Hudson River was like a winter sea, with the rustling of the leaves sounding like waves crashing. The trees were in June, but the air was in November.
[1655] The police car drove through the solemn hills, iron bridges, gravel roads, grasslands, and flower lanes. Prosecutor Bruno and Sergeant Sam were both too preoccupied to speak.

[1829] "Peruvian oil..."
[1830] "Yes, it's a widely used mucilage, mainly used in creams and ointments. By the way, it's completely harmless."
[1831] "Creams? Ointments? For what purpose, doctor?"
[1832] Dr. Shering struck his forehead heavily: "Oh, damn! I'm an idiot! I haven't dealt with this stuff in years, but I should remember that Peruvian oil is the main drug used in creams or ointments for certain skin diseases. Very common, Mr. Reen."

[1834] "Well, sometimes. But mostly it's mixed with other materials."

[1836] "I admit, at the moment..." Mr. Raine sat down, taking two minutes to ponder, then raised his head with a questioning look in his eyes. "Dr. Schelling, is there anything wrong with Mrs. Blackthorn's skin? You examined the body, so you should have noticed."
