[2029] "No, I don't think there was any original manuscript - just a rough outline of the central idea, as I said."
[2030] "Do you know where he might have kept the outline?"
[2031] She shrugged helplessly: "I can't think of anywhere else but somewhere in his laboratory."
[2032] "As for the idea itself - you said he had expressed that it was brilliant. What was his idea, Miss Black?"
[2033] "I can't say, he didn't tell me the story."
[2034] "Did Mr. Black consult your agent about this detective story?"
[2035] "I'm sure he didn't."
[2036] "How do you know?"
[2037] "I asked my agent if my father had gone to see him, and he said no."
[2038] Mr. Philo Vance stands up: "Thank you very much, Miss Blackthorn."
[2039] Scene Nine
[2040] Laboratory, June 10, Friday, 3:30 PM
[2041] Hours later, the room is silent, and Reine creeps up the attic stairs, climbing the small ladder to the roof, pushing open the trapdoor, and jumping onto the slippery roof. A detective in a raincoat and holding an umbrella stands by the chimney, exasperated. Reine greets him cheerfully, disregarding the rain dripping onto his clothes, and walks over to peer into the dark interior of the chimney. Although he knows that with a flashlight, he could see the top of the wall that separates the victim's room from the laboratory, he can see nothing now. He stands there for a moment, then waves goodbye to the detective and descends through the trapdoor, back down the stairs.
[2042] On the second floor, he looks around, all bedroom doors are open, and the hallway is empty. He quickly turns the door handle and enters the laboratory. Ned Mudd looks up from the newspaper he was reading.
[2043] "Oh, hello there!" Ned Mudd exclaims enthusiastically. "If it isn't Mr. Reine, glad to see you. I've never been assigned such a boring job."
[2044] "I believe you," Reine murmurs, his eyes darting around.
[2045] "It's so nice to see a normal face," Ned Mudd says affectionately. "It's so quiet in here – hey, hey!"
[2046] "Indeed," Raine said. "Mudd, could you do me a favor? Or rather, help your colleague on the roof. He seems quite upset and needs some company."
[2047] "Who – Claus?" Mudd asked, puzzled.
[2048] "I believe that's his name, please go up to the roof and keep him company. He seems quite upset and needs someone to talk to."
[2049] "Oh, I don't know, Mr. Raine," Mudd said, shifting uneasily. "The boss's orders are strict – I can't leave this room."
[2050] "The responsibility is mine, Mudd," Raine said impatiently. "Please go up there! And you can keep an eye on things from up there. I don't want any interruptions for the next few minutes. If anyone tries to go up to the roof, scare them away, but not too aggressively, remember."
[2051] "Er...okay, Mr. Lane," Modesty said, dragging his feet out of the laboratory.
[2052] Lane's green eyes glinted with intensity. He followed Modesty down the hallway, waiting until Modesty was out of sight before entering the adjoining room of the deceased. The room was empty, and he quickly made his way to the window overlooking the garden, checking that all the windows were locked and bolted. He returned to the door, locking the inside door first, before running out into the hallway, pulling the door up to close and lock it, and then rushing back into the laboratory, locking the door from the inside, removing his coat, and rolling up his sleeves, ready to work.
[2053] The first thing that caught his attention was the fireplace. He touched the mantelpiece, leaning down to peer under the stone arch, then straightened up, taking a few steps back...he hesitated for a moment, glancing around, before bending down and confidently passing through the outer wall of the fireplace, standing upright between the outer wall and the firewall that served as the chimney breast. The black, smooth brick wall was almost as tall as Lane's head, and Lane was a few inches over six feet tall. He took a small pencil-like flashlight from his vest pocket, sweeping the tiny beam of light over the bricks of the partition wall, hoping to find something, but finding nothing. The bricks were all neatly laid. Despite this, he tapped and prodded each brick, trying to find one that was loose. Finally, admitting that he could find nothing on this side of the laboratory, he stood up straight, estimating the height of the partition wall. Even for an elderly gentleman, it was not too difficult to climb. Considering this, he placed the pencil-like flashlight on the wall's top, grabbed the edge of the wall with his hands, and pulled himself up. He vaulted over the wall, landing on his feet with agility and grace on the bedroom side, despite being over sixty years old. His muscles were still as supple as a young man's, and he felt the raindrops from the chimney pipe lightly tapping on his head and cheeks as he vaulted over the wall.
[2054] On the bedroom side, he repeated the previous steps to search for loose bricks, but found nothing. His brow furrowed in frustration. He climbed back up to the top of the firewall, but this time he sat on it like a knight, shining his flashlight around.
[2055] He almost immediately froze, his brow clearing in relief. About a foot above his head, on the chimney pipe's wall, there was a clearly loose brick, with the mortar around it peeling off, and it protruded a bit more than the surrounding bricks. Lane's fingers clamped around the small brick, and he pulled it out with ease, almost losing his balance and falling to the ground. The brick was so loose that it came out with just a gentle tug. He carefully placed the brick on the wall's top between his legs, focusing the flashlight's beam on the dark rectangular hole in the wall.
[2056] He examined the tube closely, his eyes clouded with a grim expression.
[2057] The tube was unlabeled, filled with white liquid, and he carefully examined the hole, where there was also a dropper with a rubber bulb, but he didn't touch it. He didn't put the brick back in its place, but instead vaulted over the wall from the laboratory side, reaching down to take the white liquid-filled test tube from the wallhead, bending down to enter the laboratory.
[2058] Now his eyes were a pale green, green over gray, as if he was suffering great pain.
[2059] The tube was unlabelled and filled with a white liquid, and upon his careful examination there was another dropper with a rubber tip in the hole, but he did not touch it. Instead of putting the bricks back together, he tumbled down this side of the laboratory, reached under the wall, removed the test tube of white liquid which was resting on the head of the wall, and stooped down into the laboratory.
[2060] At the moment his eyes were a miserable green, the green masking the grey, as if he were suffering great pain.
[2061] Dirt-covered and grimacing, he tosses the tube into the pocket of the coat lying nearby and walks over to one of the charred worktables. He takes out the crumpled paper from his back pocket and slowly unfolds it. It is several sheets of thin, rough typing paper covered in neat handwriting. He reads it.
[2062] Rene often points out that this is a significant moment in the investigation of the Blackthorn case. However, from the expression on his face as he reads the document, this discovery does not lift his spirits but rather makes him more despondent. It is no wonder that his face grows increasingly grim as he reads, nodding his head gloomily from time to time, as if confirming some pre-existing conclusions; at one point, a look of utter surprise flashes across his face, but it quickly fades. After reading the entire document, he seems to hesitate before moving, as if by sitting there in extreme stillness, he can stop time, events, and the inevitable tragedy that lies ahead. But after a while, he blinks and finds a pen and paper among the clutter and begins to write furiously. He writes for a long time, laboriously copying the words from the document he found. When he is finished, he stands up, slips both the copy and the original into his back pocket, puts on his coat, shakes off the dust from his pants, and opens the laboratory door. He looks around, and the hallway is still quiet and empty.
[2063] He stands there for a long time, as still as death.
[2064] Finally, he hears a sound downstairs. He shivers slightly and walks over to the railing of the stairs. Peering through the gaps in the railing, he sees Mrs. Abigail stumbling towards the kitchen.
[2065] "Mrs. Abigail," he whispers softly.
[2066] She jumped and looked up, "Who—oh, it's you! I didn't know you were still here. What is it, sir?"
[2067] "Could you please get me a slice of bread and—oh, right, a cup of milk from the kitchen?" Raine asked cheerfully.
[2068] She stood there, staring at him, and then reluctantly nodded, wobbling out of Raine's sight. He stood there in the same unnatural stillness, waiting, and soon she returned with a tray carrying a slice of jam bread and a cup of milk, trudging up the stairs and handing the tray over the railing to Raine.
[2069] "The milk is almost gone," she blurted out, "there's only this much left."
[2070] "That's enough, thank you." As she descended the stairs with the same fierce tone, he raised the cup and began to sip the milk slowly. But as soon as she reached the bottom of the stairs and disappeared down the corridor leading to the rear of the house, Raine stopped, quickly paced back to the lab, and locked the door behind him.
[2071] At this point, he was completely clear about his next move. He placed the tray on the worktable, searching the lower cabinet under the shelves. The damage here was minimal due to the protection of the cabinet door and proximity to the floor, and he quickly found what he needed. He stood up, holding a small test tube with a cork stopper, just like the one he found in the hole. After washing the test tube under a faucet on the experimental table, he carefully poured the milk in the cup into the test tube, filling it to the same level as the white liquid in the hole. Satisfied with the similarity between the two test tubes, he corked the test tube containing the milk, poured the remaining milk into the sink, climbed back over the fireplace, sitting on the wall, and inserted the test tube containing the milk into the hole where the original test tube was found. He did not touch the pipette in the hole, then he put the yellowed stack of papers back in place, put the loose brick back in its original position, and jumped down from the wall.
[2072] He shook off the dust on his hands with disgust, wrinkling his face.
[2073] Suddenly, as if remembering something he had forgotten, he unlocked the laboratory door, returning, climbing over the brick wall separating the two fireplaces from the bedroom side, landing on the floor. He unlocked the bedroom door, entered the hallway, and entered the laboratory through the door that was no longer locked.
[2074] "Modesty!" he called out alertly to the chimney, "Modesty!"
[2075] Raindrops fell on his hot face, cooling him down.
[2076] "Yes, Mr. Rene?" came a muffled voice from the chimney pipe. Rene looked up and saw a blurry silhouette of a head in the gray frame of the chimney opening.
[2077] "Come down right away, Claus stays on the roof."
[2078] "No problem!" Modestus replied cheerfully, and his face disappeared. Soon, Modestus rushed into the laboratory.
[2079] "I'm here," he said with a satisfied smile, his suit covered in raindrops, but he seemed not to care at all, "Did you find what you were looking for?"
[2080] "Ah - forget it, Modestus," Rene said, standing still in the center of the room, "Has anyone tried to get on the roof, near the chimney?"
[2081] "No one, no movement, Mr. Raine," said Mr. Raine, his eyes wide with surprise as Raine's right hand emerged from behind him, offering a piece of bread to his mouth. Raine chewed thoughtfully, as if unaware of the madhouse of Böhme's house [note], where he had never heard of poison.
[2082] His left hand, meanwhile, was hidden in his suit pocket, tightly holding a test tube filled with a white liquid.
[2083] –
[2084] [note] Böhme: Refers to the novelist Böhme, known for his works with madcap plots.
[2085] Act III
[2086] Let me embrace you, cruel fate, for the wise say that is the wisest course of action.
[2087] Scene 1
[2088] Police Headquarters, June 10th, Friday, 5:00 PM
[2089] As Rine stepped out of Blackthorn Manor on that rainy June afternoon, he looked ten years older than when he had entered. If Sam the cop were present, he would surely wonder why Rine, who seemed so close to solving the case, appeared even more despondent than when he had encountered every obstacle. This was unlike him, as he looked so youthful at forty because he knew how to control his emotions and transform his worries into forgetfulness. However, now he looked as if all his composure and lifelong confidence had been irretrievably shattered, as if he were an old man crawling into the car.
[2090] He slumped into the car and said to Dromio, "Police Headquarters." Throughout the drive to the gray building on Central Street, a melancholic yet self-aware expression of grave concern remained on his face.
[2091] Yet, he was still himself, and as he ascended the steps to Police Headquarters, the old Jules Renardt returned, cheerful, cordial, calm, and confident in every way. The duty sergeant in the front hall recognized him and dispatched an officer to lead him to Inspector Sam's office.
[2092] It seemed to be a gloomy day, and he found the inspector, as morose as life itself, sitting in his swivel chair, staring blankly at the end of a cigar. When he saw Renardt, his face lit up, and he warmly grasped Renardt's hand. "I'm so glad to see you, Mr. Renardt. What's up?"
[2093] Renardt waved a hand and sighed, sitting down. "Any news? This place is as dead as a morgue."
[2094] Renardt nodded. "There's something that should pique your and Bruno's interest."
[2095] "Really?" Sam exclaimed. "You've already discovered –" He stopped, suspiciously looking at Renardt. "You haven't been following up on the Poirot lead, have you?"
[2096] "Pare's line?" Vance frowned. "I'm afraid I don't understand."
[2097] "Fortunately," the inspector poked the stub of his cigar into his mouth and chewed thoughtfully, "we've found something new. You know I let Pare go yesterday. Barbara Hitchcock came in and stirred things up – she hired a big lawyer – anyway, it doesn't matter, because he'll be watched all the time."
[2098] "Why? You still think Edgar Pare is involved in these cases, Inspector?"
[2099] "What do you think? What would others think? Remember, it's a frame-up – Pare's real name is Kabbian, he's Louisa's half-brother, and his father was Emily Black's first husband. Well, when I told him all I knew, he admitted it, but he was as tight as a clam. That's all I got out of him, but I didn't give up. I dug a little deeper, and you'll never guess what I found, Vance?"
[2100] "I haven't the slightest idea." Vance smiled.
[2101] "That Tom Kibian, Pere's father and the first husband of the old witch, died—"
[2102] He suddenly stopped, the smile disappeared from Mr. Rene's face, and his green-gray eyes flashed.
[2103] "So you know." Sam grumbled.
[2104] "Not from the investigation, Sergeant, but I knew it all along," Rene leaned back in his chair. "I understand your point of view. Edgar Pere Kibian, Esq., is a living topic, isn't it?"
[2105] "Why not?" Sam said pompously. "It's the truth, isn't it? Emily is responsible for the death of Pere's father—indirectly, of course, and probably not intentionally. But she killed him, just like stabbing him with a knife, all dirty work, but now we have a motive, Mr. Rene—one we didn't have before."
[2106] "That motive is —"
[2107] "Listen, you're a worldly man. A father's death by his stepmother's disease — I can understand that. The person will spend the rest of his life seeking revenge on her."
[2108] "It's basic psychology, Inspector, especially when it involves such cruel things, indeed. I can understand your consideration, that the person has a motive and an opportunity, and the intelligence to carry out a clever plan. But you have no evidence."
[2109] "That's what we're looking for."
[2110] "Meanwhile," Raine added, "I can't imagine Edgar Parrish as an action-oriented person. He's more of a planning type, but to me, he seems like the type that would easily back down in the face of violence at the last moment."
[2111] "These words are too profound for me,” the Inspector sneered, "Listen, Mr. Rene, here we are just a bunch of cops. We don't care what a person might do. We're more concerned with what the facts show he actually did."
[2112] "I insist, Inspector,” Rene calmly reiterated, "Human behavior is purely an extension of psychology. Have you noticed any suicidal intentions in Edgar Allan Poe Cabian?"
[2113] "Suicide? How could he do such a stupid thing? Of course, if we caught him red-handed…"
[2114] Rene shook his head: "No, Inspector, if Edgar Allan Poe killed someone, according to his personality, he would immediately commit suicide. Do you remember Hamlet? A weak-willed, indecisive person, yet highly intelligent in planning, while violence and plots swirled around him, he was paralyzed by guilt and remorse. But remember this: such an indecisive person, when he finally acts, he goes on a rampage and then commits suicide immediately.” Rene sighed, "I'm getting carried away again, but really, Inspector, carefully investigate this suspect, he's like the fourth act of Hamlet. In the fifth act—the plot changes, so they cannot be compared."
[2115] Sam shifted uneasily: "Well, alright, but what's your take on the whole thing?"
[2116] "I think," Lestrade suddenly laughed, "You're playing tricks, Inspector. How did you dig up this theory about Poirot again? I thought you had long forgotten it and moved on to another inspiration, and you were so careful not to let me know what it was."
[2117] Sam looked embarrassed: "Pretend I didn't say anything about inspiration. I did some investigation, but I got nothing. You haven't answered my question yet, Mr. Lestrade."
[2118] This time it was Lestrade's turn to retreat, a melancholy once again crossed his face, and his smile almost disappeared: "Honestly... I don't know what to think, Inspector."
[2119] "You mean you're stumped?"
[2120] "My meaning is, it's not the time for drastic action."
[2121] "Oh... uh, we have a lot of faith in you, Mr. Rayne, and going to the Lonsdale affair proved you have the ability to solve cases." The inspector scratched his chin. "I can say," he said somewhat awkwardly, "Bruno and us rely on you."
[2122] Rayne jumped up from his chair and began pacing back and forth: "Please, don't rely on me for anything." His anxiety was so obvious that the inspector was momentarily stunned, "Just consider that I didn't interfere with this case, Inspector. Build your own theory, please...”
[2123] Sam's face grew dark: "If you feel that way, why..."
[2124] "Yesterday - your inspiration - it didn't lead anywhere, did it?"
[2125] Sam's suspicious look did not subside: "I followed up, met with Milian."
[2126] "Ah!" Raine quickly responds, "Well, good, good, that he told you…"
[2127] "It's all things I already know from you," Sam answers somewhat awkwardly, "the vanilla thing that York Blackthorn rubbed on his arm, so you went to see the doctor too, huh?"
[2128] "Uh…yes, yes, of course." Raine suddenly sits down on a chair, covering his eyes with his hand.
[2129] Sam stares at him for a long time, very confused and angry. Then he shrugs: "Well, alright," he reluctantly says in a friendly tone, "you said you have news to tell Bruno and me, what news?"
[2130] Raine looks up: "I have an important piece of news for you, Sergeant, I need a promise from you - you cannot ask me where I got this information."
[2131] "Well, what is it?" Sam roared.
[2132] "This," he said with great caution, as if every word was carefully chosen, "before York Blackthorn disappeared, he was planning the plot of a novel."
[2133] "A novel?" Sam stared, "What of it?"
[2134] "But this is not just a novel, Inspector," Rene whispered, "It's a story he expected to write and publish someday, a detective story."
[2135] For a moment, Sam sat there, staring at Rene, his cigar hanging from his lower lip, his right temple pulsing with veins like some living thing, then he shot up from the chair, shouting, "A detective story!" The cigar fell to the ground, "Tsk, that's news!"
[2136] "Yes," Rine said solemnly, "an outline for a murder and investigation story... and there's one more thing I should tell you."
[2137] Sam was barely listening, his mind now focused, turning his vacant eyes to Rine.
[2138] "That is..."
[2139] "Ha!" Sam shook his head, then became sharp again, "What?"
[2140] "The background and characters in York Blackett's novel are real."
[2141] "Really?" the sergeant murmured, "What do you mean?"
[2142] "York Blackthorn based them directly on his own family."
[2143] The sergeant's big frame spasmed as if struck by lightning: "No," he croaked, "no, impossible, too much... absolutely—"
[2144] "Yes, Sergeant!" Raine said wearily, "You'll be interested, won't you? It's incredible, a man makes up a plot about poisoning and murder, and then it starts happening in his own home... those events, matching every step of the fictional plot exactly."
[2145] Sam drew a breath, his chest heaving like the sea: "You're telling me," he said in a deep bass, "that everything that happened at Blackthorn Manor – the two plots to poison Louisa, the murder of Mrs. Blackthorn, the fire, and the explosion – was all written down on paper, planned out as a story?" See the original text in Chinese.
[2146] "Not only that..." Raine sighed, "But, nonetheless, that's the gist of the information I've brought. I'm afraid I can't tell you any more." He rose, clutching his cane tightly, his eyes filled with helplessness and defeat. Sam paced back and forth like a wild animal, shaking his head and muttering to himself, his mind buzzing with one theory after another, then abandoning them, then deciding...
[2147] Raine walked to the door and stopped, his youthful bearing gone, his steps faltering, his once sturdy back now stooped.
[2148] The officer suddenly halted: "Wait! You said I couldn't ask questions. Fine, if you're hiding something, you must have a good reason, and I won't pry. But tell me this, every detective story has a criminal, who is the criminal in York Heat's story—if his characters are all from his own family? It's certain that whoever the criminal is in the story cannot be the actual criminal—because it's too dangerous, isn't it?"
[2149] Raine stood with his hand on the door, pondering silently: "Yes," he finally said in a lifeless voice, "Of course, you have the right to know the answer... In York Heat's murder story, the criminal is—York Heat himself."
[2150] Second Scene
[2151] Hamlet Manor, June 10, Friday, 9:00 PM
[2152] Even on that night, the usually quiet and secluded Hamlet Manor became uneasy. The rain continued to fall, accompanied by a chilling breeze that penetrated through the clothes. Standing at the peak of the cliff overlooking the Hudson River, Hamlet Manor was shrouded in layers of gray mist, with ghostly clouds hovering above, resembling a terrifying Edgar Allan Poe-style ruin.
[2153] It was a night suitable for a fire, and Old Quix had lit a huge blaze in the fireplace of Rene's sitting room. The room was warm and comfortable, and after a simple dinner, Rene lay down on the shaggy carpet in front of the fireplace, closing his eyes as the fire flickered in them. The old hunchback moved uneasily around the room, his worry and fear evident. His concern was mostly due to his keen powers of observation, as he often squinted and blinked his eyes as he observed his master, following the flickering of the flames.
[2154] "Is there something, Rene sir? Are you feeling unwell?" He cautiously approached the carpet in front of the fireplace and gently touched his master's arm. Rene's eyes, which had been closed in thought, opened wide and looked at him.
[2155] "Is there something?"
[2156] With that, Quincey retreated to a chair in the corner, sitting motionless and watching the still figure by the fire.
[2157] An hour passed in silence, until the figure finally stirred, standing up: "Quincey."
[2158] "Yes, Rayne!" the old man exclaimed, springing to his feet, his tongue half out and his expression eager.
[2159] "I'm going to the study. Do not disturb me, understand?"
[2160] "Yes, Rayne."
[2161] "If Fitzhugh or Kropotkin comes looking for me, tell them I'm asleep. They're worried about a play, no matter, I'll see them in the morning."
[2162] "Yes, Mr. Raine."
[2163] Raine patted the old hunchback's bald head and slapped his hump, urging him out. Old Quincy hesitated for a while before shuffling out, and Raine locked the door before striding confidently to the next room, his study.
[2164] He walked up to the ornate old walnut desk, turned on the desk lamp, and opened a drawer. He pulled out a stack of papers, containing the contents of the yellowed manuscript he had found in the chimney flue at Blackthorn Manor. Sitting down in the chair in front of the desk, he spread out the papers, his eyes vacant and his face somber. Then, slowly, he concentrated, reading through the outline he had hastily written that afternoon. In the quiet of the night, the words seemed to take on a new appearance. He became fully absorbed in them…
[2165] Detective Story Outline
[2166] Title (provisional): "The Vanilla Murder Mystery" by Pseudonym. The Terry Group? H. York? Louis Pasteur?
[2167] Setting: New York City's Central Park? Like my own house.
[2168] Time: Modern.
[2169] Method: First Person. I am the criminal.
[2170] Character List
[2171] York (myself) - Y. Criminal. Husband of the victim.
[2172] Emily - Victim. Elderly woman. Domineering character. (As in real life.)
[2173] Louisa - Deaf, mute, and blind daughter. (Y's stepdaughter - helpful for motive.)
[2174] Conrad - Married son, no children, unnecessary.
[2175] Martha - His wife.
[2176] Barbara - Daughter. The eldest child of Y and Emily. Maintains the writer's identity. A suspect in psychology?
[2177] Gigi - The youngest child of Y and Emily. Daughter.
[2178] Kiddett - A one-legged neighbor. Has feelings for Louisa. (Too far-fetched?)
[2179] Greer - Son's business partner.
[2180] Other characters
[2181] Lucy's nurse, butler, driver, maid, family doctor, family lawyer, Gigi's suitor?
[2182] Note! Give all the above roles pseudonyms!
[2183] First crime
[2184] Attempted poisoning of Lucy.
[2185] Fact: The household routine, the butler prepares a cup of egg and milk cocktail for Lucy every day, placed on the dining table at 2:30 PM.
[2186] Details: One day, Y (the perpetrator) waited for the butler to put the eggnog on the dining table; then, when no one was watching, Y slipped into the dining room, dropped belladonna into the eggnog, and quickly returned to the adjacent library. Y took the belladonna from the 9th bottle on the chemistry experiment shelf in his upstairs laboratory; he took three pills from that bottle. No one knew about it.
[2187] After putting the poison in the eggnog, Y waited in the library for Louisa to come drink the eggnog.
[2188] As Louisa walked towards the dining room, Y emerged from the library. Just as Louisa was about to drink the eggnog, Y entered the dining room, picked up the eggnog, saying it looked strange, and took a sip. Y immediately felt unwell. (Y designed this move to make the suspicion fall on the people around him.)
[2189] Note: This made everyone think someone wanted to poison Louisa; however, it was not Y, as the person who poisoned her could not have drunk their own poison, and this also avoided Louisa being truly poisoned – this point is crucial for the entire conspiracy.
[2190] Second crime
[2191] The second "attempt" to poison Louisa, during which time, the elderly woman Emily, Y's wife, was murdered.
[2192] Time: Seven weeks after the first poisoning attempt.
[2193] Details: In the early hours of the morning, around 4 o'clock, everyone was still sleeping, including Louisa and Emily, who were sleeping in their bedrooms (mother and daughter sharing a room, each with a single bed). Y committed his second crime.
[2194] This time, the idea was to poison a pear and place it on the fruit tray on the bedside table between Louisa and Emily's beds. Using a pear was because everyone knew that Old Emily never ate pears. Poisoning the pear would make it seem like someone else was trying to poison Louisa again, but Louisa would not eat the pear. Y knew she never ate rotten or infested fruit, so he intentionally selected (maybe stolen from the kitchen) a rotten pear and brought it into the room, injecting it with a full syringe of the poison mercuric chloride, which was taken from the laboratory - bottle number 168.
[2195] Y took the syringe from his laboratory's iron filing cabinet, where he had a whole box of syringes.
[2196] Additionally, before entering Lucy's bedroom, Y stole a pair of Conrad's old white summer shoes. And, when he filled the syringe with mercury chloride in the laboratory (just before entering Lucy's room), he deliberately spilled some poison (bottle 168) on one of Conrad's white shoes.
[2197] Action: Y sneaks into Lucy and Emily's bedroom. Walks to the bedside table and places the pear in the fruit dish. Strikes Emily on the head, killing her. (This was the real purpose of the plot, but it would appear that Emily was mistakenly killed, as if she woke up in the middle of the night, and the killer had to kill her to keep quiet.)
[2198] Note: Killing Emily was the main purpose behind the whole plot.
[2199] The act of poisoning Lucy was just to make the police think that Lucy was the intended victim. So the police would only suspect those who had a motive to kill Lucy, not Emily. In the story, Y and Lucy were very friendly, so he wouldn't be suspected.
[2200] False lead explanation: "Y deliberately spilled mercury chloride on Conrad's shoes. He put the shoes back in Conrad's closet after leaving the bedroom. The police found the poisoned shoes, which made them suspect Conrad as the poisoner, as Conrad's hatred for Lucy was well-known."
[2201] Clue that guides the police to the correct answer: Louisa is deaf, blind, and mute. The idea here is that when Y kills Emily, Louisa wakes up and smells the scent of Y's arm's eucalyptus oil - her sense of smell is her most sensitive sense, which helps the police build the clue. She later testifies to smelling the eucalyptus scent, leading the main detective to follow the clue and eventually discover the truth that Y is the only person with the eucalyptus scent.
[2202] The night after the arson murder, Y sets fire to the laboratory (which is also his bedroom). He left a bottle of carbon disulfide (Bottle No. 256) on a large table in the laboratory, which would explode when heated. Then he lit a match to burn his bed.
[2203] The purpose of the arson: The fire and subsequent explosion will make it appear as if someone also intended to kill Y. This can add another false lead, at least making Y appear innocent.
[2204] Third crime
[2205] Two weeks after the murder, Y "attempts" to "poison" Louisa again. This time he uses a poison called atropine, which is a white liquid taken from his experimental shelf, Bottle No. 220. Louisa drinks a glass of skim milk every hour after dinner, and Y drops fifteen drops of it into her skim milk using an eye dropper. Once again, Y either draws Louisa's attention to the fact that the skim milk is off or finds a way to prevent Louisa from drinking the poisoned skim milk.
[2206] Purpose: At any point, this scheme was never intended to cause Louisa's death. The third attempt after her death was only to continue to make the police believe that the killer still wanted to kill Louisa, so they would investigate those who had a motive to kill Louisa rather than Emily.
[2207] General notes
[2208] (1) Remember that Y always wore gloves, so there were no fingerprints left on any object during any of the crimes.
[2209] (2) Thoroughly plan the main plot.
[2210] (3) Thoroughly plan how the protagonist detective finally solves the case.
[2211] (4) Y's motive: Hate Emily - she ruined his career - his health - controlled and destroyed him... enough to trigger a real murder!
[2212] The last remark, irrelevant to the novel and bitterly sarcastic, was heavily crossed out with a pencil (Raine faithfully copied the original) but still legible. The novel outline ended with two remaining points of note.
[2213] (5) Make sure all characters are disguised to look like fictional characters. If using pseudonyms and using fake names for all characters, the general public should not recognize them as my family. Perhaps change the setting to another city, such as Chicago or San Francisco.
[2214] (6) What is the personality of the protagonist detective? A doctor because of the vanilla and chemical items involved? Y's friend? Not a typical detective. Use deductive reasoning - a smart detective; perhaps with the appearance of Sherlock Holmes, the flair of Hercule Poirot, E.Q.'s deductive methods... make the laboratory prominent in the investigation... create a trail by labeling the bottles in the laboratory.
[2215] Raine's thin, tense face tightened as he dropped the disorganized detective novel outline by York Blackthorn, resting his head in his hands. He sat in silence, pondering.
[2216] Fifteen minutes passed with no other sound but his own faint breathing.
[2217] Finally, he sat up straight and looked at the calendar in the corner of his desk. His lips moved slightly. Two weeks...
[2218] He picked up a pencil and with heavy, almost desperate strokes, circled June 18th.
[2219] Third Scene
[2220] Morgue, June 11th, Saturday, 11:00 a.m.
[2221] A force was compelling him. Someone so accustomed to rigorous self-reflection and sharp analysis of the world around him was entangled by this inextricable and inexplicable emotion. He couldn't fully analyze it or explain it. Reason was useless here, it felt like a sack of pencils pressing on his neck.
[2222] Yet he couldn't just let it go. He had to get to the bottom of this - the pain that would surely follow, he knew all too well. And what then... His heart sank, and his stomach churned with sorrow and fear.
[2223] It was Saturday, the sun's rays blazing down on the river, as he stepped out of his Lincoln and walked down the sidewalk, his steps heavy as he ascended the worn stone steps of the morgue. Why was he here? Why not admit that his nature was too delicate and sensitive for such an unscrupulous and unethical profession? He had faced equal parts of humiliation and praise during the peak of his stage career. Titles ranging from "world-class actor" to "outdated old man reciting Shakespearean relics in the age of new technology" had been bestowed upon him. He had accepted them all, mocking or applauding, with dignity, for he was a discerning artist. No matter what the critics, who rose to prominence with a new art movement, said, his unwavering goal, his belief in fulfilling a meaningful mission, would not be shaken. Why didn't he just stop, at the pinnacle of his career, when he had reached completion? Why wade into this murky waters? Hunting down the guilty is Sam and Bruno's job. What is evil? There is no such thing as pure evil, even Satan was once an angel. There is no real evil, only ignorance or distorted people, or victims of fate.
[2224] His bony legs climbed the stairs of the morgue involuntarily, embracing a new mission of investigation and verification, stubbornly refusing the turbulent struggle that raged in his mind.
[2225] He stood in a laboratory on the second floor, staring blankly at the rows of neatly arranged glass and metal instruments, reading Morgue's sharp lecture silently, observing his deft hands in action.

[2228] "Curare? No problem!" The expert in toxicology smiles, "We are very familiar with it. It is a white, odorless, toxic bi-salt - a deadly poison, the daddy of the alkaloids. Its chemical structure is C15H21N3O2 - derived from Calabar bean."
[2229] "Calabar bean?" Reine repeats blankly.
[2230] "The source of curare. Calabar bean is the seed of a climbing African plant in the legume family, containing a deadly poison," Doctor Mergus explains, "Medically, it is used to treat certain specific neurological disorders, muscle stiffness, spasms, and dementia. Curare is extracted from this bean. Rats, and almost all other animals, will die if they eat it. Would you like to see a sample?"
[2231] "No need, doctor," Reineck said, pulling a tightly wrapped object from his pocket and tearing off the packaging and padding. It was a stoppered test tube with a white liquid inside that he had found in the chimney secret chamber. "Is this belladonna?"
[2232] "Hmm," Meigs pondered, holding the test tube up to the light. "It looks like it, wait, Mr. Reineck, let me do a few tests."
[2233] He worked in silence, and Reineck watched without interrupting.
[2234] "Yes, it is," the toxicology expert said finally. "It's definitely belladonna, Mr. Reineck, and it's potent. Where did you get it?"
[2235] "From Blackthorn Manor," Reineck replied vaguely. He took out his wallet and searched through it until he found a folded piece of paper. "This," he said, "is a copy of the prescription, Doctor Meigs. Can you take a look at it?"
[2236] The toxicologist took the prescription: "Hmm... Peruvian oil... Ah, I see! What do you want to know, Mr. Reine?"
[2237] "Is this prescription legal?"
[2238] "Oh! Of course, a compound ointment for treating skin conditions..."
[2239] "Thank you," Reine said wearily, not bothering to take back the prescription, "And... could you do something for me, doctor?"
[2240] "Go ahead."
[2241] "Send this vial to the police headquarters in my name, to be archived with the other evidence in the Blackthorn case."
[2242] "No problem."
[2243] "This should," Reine explained gravely, "be entered into the official records. It's crucial to this case... Thank you for your help, doctor."
[2244] He shook Mishug's hand and turned towards the door, the toxicology expert watching him leave with an astonished gaze.
[2245] Fourth scene
[2246] Detective Sam's office, June 16, Thursday, 10:00 a.m.
[2247] It seemed the case was destined to have a resolution. The case that began with conspiracy and violence, seemingly purposeful and bold, had swept through the Blackthorn family with one scandalous crime after another, and suddenly fell silent today, as if after a long build-up of energy, it had accidentally hit an impassable barrier and shattered into pieces, unable to move an inch further.
[2248] This was a testing period. Since Rine's visit to Meigs's laboratory, six days had passed without incident. Detective Sam found himself at a dead end, spinning in circles without any progress. The Blackthorn mansion seemed to return to normal, meaning its inhabitants resumed their peculiar lifestyle without any interference from the police, who were powerless to do anything. For an entire week, the media was filled with negative reports, such as one newspaper saying that the "mad Blackthorn family" had somehow escaped "this latest prank" unscathed. "Another tragic example," a news editor remarked solemnly, "of the growing crime in America, where murder seems to be getting away with it, and with impunity."

[2264] "Exactly."

To be honest with you, Bruno and I have a lot of respect for your abilities, Mr. Raine, and frankly - you have something, I don't know what, that Bruno and I don't have, and we know it. Otherwise, we wouldn't let an outsider run wild like this."

But how could you be sure that in the plot, York Blackthorn is the criminal? So that's not an option either. I admit I'm stuck, God, I hate this feeling!"
[2274] Mr. Raine shifted in his seat, sighed, and his pained expression was mistaken for impatience, "This logic is terrible, Inspector, forgive me for saying so, but I can't discuss it with you any further."

[2333] Fifth scene
[2334] Hamlet Manor, June 17, Friday, afternoon
[2335] Early in the morning, Officer Sam drove a black sedan with Pire to Hamlet Manor, explaining that the Blackthorns believed Pire would be interrogated all day and then drove away immediately.
[2336] Now in his own territory, René felt at ease with every blade of grass and tree. He and the tutor strolled through the estate, chatting happily about his theater, his books, his garden - everything except the Blackt family. Piri was infected by the extraordinary beauty of the surroundings and became more open. He took deep breaths of the air like wine, his eyes lit up when he entered the Mermaid Tavern, where history had placed great value, and examined a first folio edition of Shakespeare in a glass case - lost in himself, as if he were a different person.
[2337] René calmly led him around, his gaze fixed on the man's expressions, gestures, and every movement. He studied Piri's lip movements and the way he opened and closed his mouth, his posture, his gait, every detail of his movements. At lunch, he watched Piri's eating habits. Quixi followed closely, like a deformed little vulture studying the tutor's head. Around mid-afternoon, Quixi disappeared.
[2338] In the afternoon, they continued to stroll through the vast estate, but René began to subtly steer the conversation towards Piri. Soon, the talk became very personal. René delved into the man's tastes, prejudices, ideas, and the essence of Barbara Blackt's intellectual circle, his relationships with the Blackt family members, and the content of the children's lessons. During this time, Piri became lively again, telling him where to find books, his individual teaching methods for the boy, and his daily routine at the Blackt home.
[2339] After dinner, the two men went to Quixi's small laboratory. It was a strange place that Piri had never seen before. Although there were modern appliances, it exuded an ancient atmosphere, looking like a medieval torture chamber. One wall had a row of shelves with a line of human heads - Mongolian, Caucasian, African, and so on - with all kinds of facial expressions and appearances; wigs - gray, black, brown, red, fluffy, curly, straight, dry, shiny, and curly - hung on several walls. The worktable was piled high with various pigments, powders, creams, dyes, adhesives, and small metal instruments. There was a sewing machine-like machine, a multi-faceted mirror, a giant spotlight, and black screens - ... From the moment Piri stepped through the door, his lively spirit disappeared, and the old fear and hesitation returned to his face. The laboratory seemed to dampen his spirits and bring him back to the real world, and suddenly he became silent and awkward. René anxiously observed him, and Piri nervously looked around, and his distorted projections followed him.

[2380] Act VI
[2381] The deceased's room, June 18, Saturday evening, 8:20 p.m.

[2388] "I hear," Renard said, sitting down, "that the ban will be lifted this morning, and the police will be withdrawn."
[2389] Blackthorn murmured, "Oh, it's about time. There's not even anything worth cursing about. We've been stuck in the same spot since my mother was found murdered two weeks ago."
[2390] Renard made a grimace. "We're not perfect, you know... ah, they're here. Good morning, Nero."
[2391] "Good morning, Mr. Rainey," said Modd, his footsteps as big as an elephant as he entered the library, "Well, sir, we have to go now. Mr. Blackthorn!"
[2392] "Mr. Rainey just told me."
[2393] "It's the chief's order. We're leaving - just waiting for the clock to strike noon. Sorry, Mr. Blackthorn."
[2394] "Sorry?" Blackthorn repeated, rising with a grumpy wave of his arms, "Fine, get out of here! We can finally enjoy some peace and quiet."
[2395] "And privacy," added a grumbling voice as Jilly Blackthorn entered the room, "We've been disturbed enough, Conrad, we can finally have some peace."
[2396] The four men stationed in the room - Nix, Pike, Klaus, and a young man with black hair, Dr. Dubin, the expert sent to test the poison in the food - all gathered at the door.
[2397] "Well, boys," Pike said, "let's go. I have an appointment, ha, ha!" He burst into a boisterous laugh, then suddenly choked on his words and the laughter died in his throat. He stared wide-eyed at Rine's seat.
[2398] Everyone turned to look. Mr. Rine slumped in his chair, his eyes closed, his face devoid of color - unconscious.
[2399] Dr. Dubin immediately rushed forward, Pike gaped, "He just stiffened up! His face turned red, he coughed a little, and then he collapsed!"
[2400] The poison expert knelt beside the chair, yanked open Rine's collar, and bent over to listen to Rine's heartbeat with his ear pressed against his chest. His face was grim: "Water," he whispered, "and whiskey, immediately."
[2401] Jirou leaned against the wall, dumbfounded; Konrad Hett murmured incoherently, took a bottle of whiskey from the cabinet; a police officer ran to the kitchen and brought back a glass of water. Dr. Dobin forced open Ren's mouth and poured a large gulp of whiskey down his throat; the police officer, eager to help, poured a full glass of water over Ren's face.
[2402] The effect was immediate. Ren's throat gurgled, his eyes rolled wildly, and he coughed violently as the whiskey went down.
[2403] "Idiot!" Dr. Dobin growled, "What are you doing - trying to kill him? Come here - help me... Mr. Hett, can you put him somewhere? He needs to be in bed right away. Heart attack..."
[2404] "Are you sure it's not poisoning?" Jirou gaped. Barbara, Martha, the two children, and Mrs. Abuqo all rushed over.
[2405] "Good heavens," Barbara exclaimed, "What happened to Mr. Ren?"
[2406] "Can anyone help me?" Dr. Dobin gasps, struggling to lift Laine's limp body from the chair.
[2407] A roar comes from down the hall, and the people blocking the door scatter as Red-haired Dromio bursts in…
[2408] Less than fifteen minutes later, the house is quiet again. Dr. Dobin and Dromio carry the weak Laine up to the third-floor bedroom. The three detectives stand motionless, uncertain of what to do next, and eventually, with no instructions to revoke their orders, they leave the mansion, leaving Laine and the Het family to fend for themselves. After all, a heart attack and a murder case are unrelated.
[2409] The rest of the people gather outside the closed door of the bedroom. Nothing can be heard from the outside, and suddenly the door opens, and Dromio's fiery head appears: "The doctor has asked you all to stay away and make no noise!"
[2410] The door slams shut.
[2411] So they slowly dispersed. Half an hour later, Dr. Dobbin emerged, descending the stairs.
[2412] "Complete silence and rest are required," he informed them. "It's not serious, but he cannot be moved for one or two days. Please do not disturb him; his driver will accompany and take care of him until he can leave. I will return tomorrow—he will be much better by then."
[2413] At 7:30 pm, Mr. Jury Rene began his task of creating an opportunity for his "heart attack." With Dr. Dobbin's strict instructions, no one dared approach the "sickroom." Indeed, Barbara had called Dr. Merrion's office for a consultation—perhaps out of some inexplicable unease—but when she learned that the doctor was out of town, she did not take any further action.
[2414] Dromio settled comfortably inside the door, enjoying a cigar and a magazine, and found that the afternoon had passed quite pleasantly, judging by the relaxed expression on Rene's face. He seemed to be much more comfortable than his boss.
[2415] At six o'clock, Barbara instructed Mrs. Abigail to prepare a light meal to be sent to the bedroom. Dromio, with the bravado of a Gael, gladly accepted it, saying that Rene was recuperating and then closing the door in Mrs. Abigail's displeased face. Soon after, Miss Smith knocked on the door out of professional duty, inquiring if there was anything she could do. Dromio discussed the situation with her for five minutes, and though the conversation was pleasant, it was clear that he was being rejected. She shook her head and walked away.
[2416] At 7:30, Mr. Jury got up, whispered a few words to Dromio, and stood behind the door. Dromio opened the door and peered out. The hallway was empty, so he closed the door behind him and walked down the corridor. Miss Smith's door was open, and no one was inside, and the doors to the laboratory and nursery were closed, and Dromio quickly returned to the guest room after checking that no one was in the room.
[2417] Soon after, Mr. Jury tiptoed down the hallway and quickly entered the dead man's room.
[2418] He hesitated for a moment before opening the closet door and slipping inside, closing the door just enough to watch the room. The hallway, the entire second floor, and the room itself were silent. The room soon grew dark, and the closet was stuffy, but Reen still burrowed deeper into the pile of women's clothes, striving to breathe and prepare for the long night ahead.
[2419] Time passed slowly. Occasionally, Dromio would bow to the door of the guest room and listen to the muffled sounds from the hallway and downstairs; Reen, on the other hand, had no sense of the outside world, as it was pitch black and no one entered the room where he was hiding. Reen knew from his nightwatch that it was 7:50, and there were signs of movement outside for the first time.
[2420] Suddenly, the room was illuminated, and he guessed that the light switch was on the other side of the closet door, so he couldn't see the visitor coming in. But the suspense didn't last long, and Miss Smith's bulky figure passed by his line of sight, her heavy footsteps across the carpet, turning towards the two single beds. Now that the lights were on, Reen could see clearly, and the room had been thoroughly cleaned, ventilated, and tidied, and all traces of the crime had been removed.
[2421] Miss Smith walked to the bedside table, picked up Louisa Cabeen's point chart and blocks. She turned around, and Renet saw her face. She looked tired, and her wide chest heaved with a sigh. She didn't do anything else and left Renet's sight, heading towards the door. The lights went out, and Renet was plunged back into darkness.
[2422] He sighed with relief, wiping the sweat from his forehead.
[2423] At 8:05, a second visitor came to the deceased's room. The lights came on again, and Renet saw the tall, frail figure of Mrs. Abigail passing across the carpet. She was panting heavily, and Renet assumed it was from climbing the stairs. She made a displeased face, rubbed her neck, and then turned and walked out.
[2424] However, this time - Renet murmured a few incoherent prayers, thanking the gods great and small for Mrs. Abigail's carelessness - the light was not turned off.
[2425] And then, almost immediately after, at 8:09, Renet realized that the window on the wall opposite the door had moved slightly. He tensed up, bent his body lower, held his breath, and widened the gap in the wardrobe door slightly, keeping a close eye on the window.
[2426] Suddenly, the blinds that had been lowered were pulled up, and he saw the person he had been waiting for, crawling on the windowsill that overlooked the garden, spanning the entire exterior of the second floor. The person remained there for a few seconds before quickly jumping into the room. Rayne saw that the window that had been closed was now open. The figure leaped towards the direction of the door, out of Rayne's sight, but he was sure the visitor was closing the door, as the person soon returned, and the lights remained on. The figure moved towards the fireplace, and Rayne could only see a part of it. The person bent slightly and disappeared, and then he saw two legs lifting up before disappearing. Rayne's heart raced as he waited.
[2427] A few seconds later, the figure reappeared, holding the white liquid tube and dropper that Rayne had left in the secret hole behind the bricks.
[2428] The visitor crossed the room and went to the bedside table. His eyes gleamed, and his hand reached for the skimmed milk. Hiding in the closet, Rayne shuddered. A brief hesitation... and then, as if making up his mind, the person unscrewed the bottle, pouring the entire contents of the tube into the skimmed milk sent by Mrs. Abigail.
[2429] The action was so quick... The person leaped back to the window edge, glanced quickly at the garden, and flipped over the window sill - the windows and blinds were all pulled down again. Rayne noticed that the visitor left the blinds slightly higher than before... He sighed in the closet, stretching his legs, and his face was serious.
[2430] The whole process took no more than three minutes, and Rayne looked at his watch, which was now exactly 8:12.
[2431] Intermission... Nothing happened, and the blinds did not move at all. Rayne wiped his forehead again, and sweat dripped down his body into his clothes.
[2432] At 8:15, Rayne had a feeling that someone had come. Two silhouettes momentarily blocked the light passing through his line of sight – Lucy Cabian, as she usually walked around the house, slowly and confidently, followed by Miss Smith. Lucy walked straight to her bed, sat down, crossed her legs, and mechanically, as if it was a nightly routine, reached out to the bedside table and grabbed the glass of skimmed milk. Miss Smith smiled faintly and patted her cheek before walking to the right – to the bathroom, Rayne knew, recalling the layout of the room.
[2433] What caught Rayne's attention was not Lucy but the window through which the intruder had escaped. As Lucy raised the glass cup behind her, Rayne saw a sneaky shadow, a ghostly face, pressed against the window glass that the blinds did not cover. The face was tense and pale, intensely focused – almost frightening...
[2434] And yet, Lucy remained calm, her expression unchanged, as she drank the skimmed milk in the glass, put down the cup, stood up, and began to undress.
[2435] At this moment, Rayne's eyes ached from staring so intently. He could swear that the face at the window, first showed an expression of incredulity, then one of dreadful disappointment. Then, the figure disappeared like a toy.
[2436] While Miss Smith was still in the bathroom, Rene quietly stepped out of the closet and slipped out of the room. Lulu didn't even turn her head.
[2437] Scene 7
[2438] Laboratory, June 19, Sunday, afternoon
[2439] On Sunday morning, Mr. Philo Rene felt much better - much better than the day before. However, Domerio informed the only person in the house who seemed to care about Rene - Miss Barbara Hite - that Mr. Rene would stay in the guest room for some time in the morning and afternoon, and if Miss Hite could kindly ask everyone not to disturb him?
[2440] Miss Hite agreed, and Mr. Philo Rene would not be disturbed.
[2441] At eleven o'clock, Dr. Dupin comes to visit and meets with the "patient" in private for ten minutes before reporting that the "patient" is largely recovered and leaving.
[2442] Later in the afternoon, Renard repeats his mysterious investigation from the previous night. Even if he is truly ill, his complexion should not be so pale, and he looks emaciated, having not slept the previous night. Dromio signals him, and he quickly walks down the hallway. However, this Sunday reconnaissance mission is not to the room of the deceased, but rather to the laboratory. He has planned ahead, hiding behind the door to the left of the entrance as soon as he enters, leaving a gap in the door to monitor through. He waits calmly again.
[2443] This action seems both crazy and insignificant. Hunched over in a dark and stuffy space, it's difficult to breathe and his eyes strain from constantly peering through the gap - endlessly waiting, hours pass with nothing happening, no one enters the laboratory, and no movement is seen.
[2444] This day seems endless.
[2445] Regardless of the anger, boiling thoughts, and tormenting ideas in his head, he allows himself no second of relaxation. Finally, at four o'clock in the afternoon, his wait has a result.
[2446] His first instinct was that a figure came from the direction of the door he could not see, flashing past his eyes. Of course, Lain could not hear the sound of the door opening and closing. The fatigue of long waiting disappeared, and his eyes fixed on the gap.
[2447] It was the intruder from the previous night.
[2448] The intruder walked confidently towards the wall shelf in the direction of the door to the left, stopping at a position so close to Lain that he could smell the other's breath.
[2449] The intruder reached out with both hands to a lower shelf – taking a bottle that had not been broken off the shelf, lowering it with the bottle. Lain saw the white letters on the red label: poison. Clear and obvious. The intruder paused for a moment, silently examining the loot in his hand; then, after slowly surveying the room, he made his way to the pile of broken glass in the corner of the room by the window, picked up an unbroken small bottle, filled it with poison, corked it, put the bottle of poison he had taken from the shelf back on the shelf, and then crept towards Lain's direction... for a moment, Lain stared intently into the burning eyes of the intruder... and then the eyes passed him by, heading towards the door. Lain sat in a weary posture for a long time, then got up and quickly stepped into the laboratory. The door was closed, and the intruder was nowhere to be found.
[2450] He also did not go to the wall shelf to see what poison the intruder had stolen. He merely stood there quietly, like an old man carrying a thousand-pound burden, blankly staring at the door.
[2451] Then, the pain disappeared, and he was back to being the usual Raine, a little pale and hunched, like someone who had just recovered from a heart attack. He followed the intruder's path, albeit a little weak, but confidently left the room.
[2452] Police headquarters, night.
[2453] The headquarters was quiet. It was after hours, and except for the night shift police, the corridors were empty. Bruno prosecutor strode down the hallway, knocking on the door with the nameplate "Inspector Sam" written on it.
[2454] Sam sat at his desk, reading the criminal photo album under a table lamp.
[2455] "How's it going, Sam?" Bruno called out.
[2456] Sam didn't even look up: "What do you mean?"
[2457] "Rene! Any news?"
[2458] "Nothing."
[2459] "I'm worried," Bruno growled. "You shouldn't have agreed to this crazy idea, Sam. Canceling protection for these people could lead to a tragedy..."
[2460] "Oh, go sell your protective orders elsewhere," Sam snarled. "What do we have to lose? Rene seems to know what he's doing, and we have no clue."
[2461] Bruno shook his head: "I still think this is unwise, what if something goes wrong…"
[2462] "Hey, listen!" Sam bellowed, his little eyes blazing with anger. "Do I have enough troubles already, and do I have to listen to this old hag's nonsense—"
[2463] He bit his lip and jumped, startled by the ringing of the phone on the table. Bruno tensed.
[2464] Sam picked up the receiver.
[2465] "Hello," he grunted.
[2466] A flurry of excited chatter… Sam listened, a blush spreading across his face.
[2467] Then, with a bang, he hung up the phone and rushed out the door.
[2468] Baffled, Bruno had no choice but to follow.
[2469] Act 8
[2470] Restaurant, June 19, Sunday, 7:00 pm
[2471] That afternoon, Mr. Philo Vance had strolled around the house, smiling and chatting with the various members of the household. Earlier, Gregg had dropped by, and Vance had chatted with him for a while. Captain Van Derveer had spent the entire afternoon in the garden with Louisa Kibbey and Miss Smith. The others were listless and lacked concentration, unable to do anything normal, and still half-suspicious of each other.
[2472] Notably, Vance had not sat down once throughout the house. He paced, alertly watching, tracking, and monitoring…
[2473] At fifteen minutes to seven, he subtly signaled to his driver, Dromio. Dromio slipped over to him, and they whispered for a moment before Dromio slipped out of the house and returned five minutes later, smiling.
[2474] At seven, Vance sat in a corner of the dining room, smiling affably. The dinnerware had been set, and the family entered the dining room one by one, looking just as tired and listless as before. At that moment, Sam, accompanied by Bruno the prosecutor and a squad of detectives, suddenly arrived.
[2475] As Vance rose to greet Sam and Bruno, his smile faded. For a moment, no one moved. Louisa and Miss Smith sat at the table, Martha Blackthorne and the two children were about to sit down, and Barbara had just entered from another door. Konrad was in the adjoining library, as usual, guzzling liquor. Gilbert was not present, but Captain Van Derveer and John Gregg were there, standing behind Louisa's chair.
[2476] No one spoke until Renet quietly said, "Ah, Inspector." Only then did the shocked expressions of the others fade away, and they sat down in silence.
[2477] Sam barked a greeting and followed Bruno to Renet, nodding solemnly to him. The three of them retreated to a corner, without anyone paying attention to them. The diners unfolded their napkins, and Mrs. Abigail entered. The maid, Vittoria, entered with a heavy tray...
[2478] "How is it?" Sam asked, rather calmly.
[2479] A tired and emaciated expression returned to Renet's face: "That's it, Inspector." He only replied with this, and the three of them fell silent for a moment.
[2480] Then the inspector shouted, "Your men - he just called me on the phone - told me you were giving up, washing your hands of it."
[2481] Bruno croaked, "You failed?"
[2482] "Yes," Rine whispered, "I failed. I'm giving up, gentlemen. The experiment…didn't succeed."
[2483] Sam and Bruno said nothing, only staring at him.
[2484] "I can't do anything else," Rine continued, his gaze seeming to fall on something behind Sam. "I notified you because I'm returning to Harleth House, and I can't leave without your man back on guard—to protect the Blackthorns…"
[2485] "So, you're defeated too," Sam repeated, his voice harsh.
[2486] "Fearfully, I suppose," Lian shrugged, "I was full of hope this afternoon, and now... I'm beginning to believe, Inspector, that I overestimated my abilities. I think I was just lucky with the Longsden case last year."
[2487] Bruno sighed, "It's too late to mourn. After all, we're not doing much better. You don't have to be so upset."
[2488] Sam shook his head gravely, "Bruno's right. Don't take this too seriously. You should be happy, knowing someone's with you…" He suddenly fell silent, turning around like an overgrown cat, staring in terror at what lay behind Lian.
[2489] It happened so quickly that they were caught off guard, and before they could even take a breath, it was over. It was like a bolt of lightning, like a snake's bite, instantly paralyzing them.
[2490] The Blackthorns and their guests sat around the table, frozen in terror. Little Jock, who had been banging the table for more bread, picked up the glass of milk in front of him—there were several glasses on the table: one for Jock, one for Billy, and one for Lucy—and greedily gulped down half of it. The glass slipped from his fingers, and he suddenly went limp, only shuddering once as he choked. After that, Jock stiffened and collapsed onto the chair, then fell to the floor with a thud. They snapped out of their paralysis and rushed to his side—Sam and Lian at the same time, with Bruno close behind. The others were frozen in shock, their mouths agape and their hands motionless, holding forks in mid-air, reaching for salt that stopped midway. Mrs. Blackthorn let out a scream and fell to her knees beside the motionless body.
[2491] "He's poisoned! He's poisoned! Oh, my God... Jody, talk to me!”
[2492] Sam roughly pushed her aside, holding Jody's chin, squeezing until his mouth opened, and then stuck a finger down his throat. A weak gurgle... "Don't move, everyone!” Sam yelled, "Call the doctor, Mr. Nesbitt! He –"
[2493] The command was cut short as the small body in his arms suddenly jerked forward and then went limp, like a wet rag.
[2494] Even his shocked mother could see that the boy was dead.
[2495] Same location, 8:00 PM
[2496] Upstairs in the nursery, Dr. Merian paced back and forth - having just returned from his weekend trip an hour before the tragedy. Mrs. Blackthorn sobbed hysterically, clutching her youngest son Billy tremblingly to her chest, crying over his dead brother - clinging to her fearfully. The Blackthorn family stood around the still body, silent, somber, and avoiding eye contact. A group of detectives stood at the threshold...
[2497] Downstairs in the dining room, two people - Inspector Sam and Jerry Renn, the latter's eyes filled with pain and a sickly complexion - a look that even his acting skills could not conceal.
[2498] They didn't speak. Renn slumped at the table, staring at the floor.
[2499] The empty milk cup lay on the ground, the last sip of Socrates poison consumed by the dead boy; Sam paced back and forth, seething and muttering.
[2500] The door opened, and District Attorney Bruno stumbled in.
[2501] "A mess," he mutters, "a mess, a mess."
[2502] Sam glares at Renn, who remains unmoving, idly playing with the tablecloth.
[2503] "We'll never forget this, Sam," Bruno roars.
[2504] "Damn it!" Sam roars, "What pisses me off the most is that he had to give up now, now, why, buddy, why can't you give up now?"
[2505] "I have to," Renn simply replies, "I have to, Sam." He rises stiffly from the table, "I have no right to interfere anymore. The boy's death…" He licks his dry lips, "No, I shouldn't have gotten involved in the first place. Let me go."
[2506] "But Mr. Ren…" Bruno began weakly.
[2507] "I can't say anything to justify myself. I've made the most terrible mess, the boy's death is my fault, it's… all my fault. No…"
[2508] "Well," Sam said quietly, his anger subsiding, "you have the right to retire, Mr. Ren. If there's anything to blame, it'll be on me. If you want to walk away like this, without explaining anything, without pointing out what you've been investigating…"
[2509] "But I've told you," Ren said flatly, "I've told you. I was wrong, that's it, wrong."
[2510] "No," Bruno said, "you can't just walk away like that, Mr. Ren. There's something deeper here. When you asked Sam to pull his men back and give you free rein, you had a clear idea in your mind…"
[2511] "That was indeed the case," Bruno suddenly noticed, startled, that Rain's eyes were darkened, "I thought I could prevent further conspiracies, but it turned out I couldn't."
[2512] "All these tricks," Sam shouted, "you said so firmly that poisoning was a distraction, and it wasn't true, there wasn't much truth in it!" He roared, covering his cheeks with his hands, "Tell you, this proves that a wholesale massacre is happening here, they, that group of people, are doomed to be wiped out…"
[2513] Rain lowered his head mournfully, hesitating to speak, then walked to the door. He didn't even take his hat. Outside, he paused for a moment, as if hesitating whether to turn back, then straightened his chest and left the house. Dromio waited for him on the sidewalk. A group of reporters rushed towards him in the half-dazed night.
[2514] He shook them off, stepped into the car, and as the car sped away, his face was buried in his hands.
[2515] -
[2516] Note: Socratic drug - refers to the "Socratic irony" method of feigning ignorance to make the other party reveal the truth
[2517] Conclusion
[2518] One demon has passed, but many evils remain.
[2519] Two months have passed.
[2520] Since Philo Reyne stepped out of Blackthorn Manor, his association with the case came to an end, and there was no further news from Hamlet Manor, and neither Inspector Sam or Prosecutor Buppno contacted Reyne again.
[2521] The press has harshly criticized the police for their handling of the case, and the mention of Vance's involvement has disappeared due to lack of evidence. Two months have passed without any further investigation or developments in the case.
[2522] An internal investigation will take place, and while Sam Spade suffered some injuries in the process, the threat of demotion has been dropped. As a result, the police are forced to withdraw from Blackthorn Manor entirely.
[2523] Behind the Scenes
[2524] First, a thorough review of the situation is conducted to determine whether Vance's accomplishments can be denied.
[2525] When Old Quincannon appeared in the hallway, followed by Sam Spade and Bruno Velde, Philo Vance was lying on the grassy edge of the pool, feeding his black swans bread crumbs.
[2526] Both appeared somewhat shy and withdrawn. Quincannon tapped Renet's shoulder, and Renet turned around, immediately jumping up with infinite surprise.
[2527] "Inspector Sam! Mr. Bruno!" he exclaimed.
[2528] "It's good to see you," Sam murmured, hesitantly approaching, "Bruno and I came to visit you."
[2529] "Ah, yes," Bruno said.
[2530] They stood there awkwardly, unsure of what to do next.
[2531] Rene shrewdly surveyed them: "Sit down with me on the grass," he finally said. He was dressed in shorts and a pullover, his muscular, tanned legs covered in green grass, sitting cross-legged like an Indian.
[2532] Bruno took off his coat, unbuttoned his collar, and sighed contentedly, then sat down. The inspector hesitated, then sat down with the force of Mount Olympus. They sat in silence for a long time. Rene fixedly gazed at the pond, and a beautiful swan with a long neck came to the surface to pick up a piece of bread crumbs.
[2533] "Uh,” finally Sam spoke, “It's really... hey!" He reached out to pat Rene's arm, and Rene turned to look at him. "I'm talking to you, Mr. Rene!" he said.
[2534] "Yes," Rene murmured, "go on."
[2535] "I'll just tell you," Sam said, blinking his eyes, "we - Bruno and I, I mean - we want to ask you something."
[2536] "Whether Louisa Cabian died naturally?"
[2537] They were startled, looking at each other. Then Bruno leaned forward: "Yes," he said eagerly, "did you notice the news in the paper? We were considering reopening the old case... What do you think?"
[2538] Sam remained silent, his gaze fixed tightly on Raine under his furrowed brow.
[2539] "I thought," Raine murmured, "that Dr. Thorn agreed with Dr. Mira's diagnosis of heart failure."
[2540] "Well," the sergeant said slowly, "he did agree, at least, Dr. Mira had always claimed that the deaf and dumb girl's heart was not good, and that was recorded in his records, but we were not so sure..."
[2541] "We think," the prosecutor said, "there may have been some undetectable poison or injection that could have caused death without arousing suspicion."
[2542] "But I told you two months ago," Rein replied mildly, tossing another crumb into the water, "that I've washed my hands of it."
[2543] "We know," Bruno quickly said before Sam could blurt out a reply, "but we couldn't help feeling that you've held onto some evidence..."
[2544] He stopped. Rein had turned his head away, a smile still on his lips, but his green eyes thoughtful, looking past the swans. After a long moment, he sighed and turned back to his guests.
[2545] "You're right," he said.
[2546] Sam pulled a handful of grass from the ground and threw it at Raine's feet. "I knew it!" he roared. "Bruno, didn't I tell you? He had something, something we could use—"
[2547] "The case is closed, Inspector," said Raine calmly.
[2548] Both men were startled, and Sam gripped Raine's arm so tightly that the inspector instinctively recoiled.
[2549] "Closed? Who? What? When? When, for heaven's sake—last week?" he croaked.
[2550] "The case was closed two months ago."
[2551] For a moment, they were both too stunned to speak. Then Bruno gasped loudly, his face turning pale; Sam's upper lip trembled like a child's.
[2552] "So you mean," Sam whispered finally, "for two months, you've been keeping quiet, letting the killer get away?"
[2553] "The killer isn't getting away."
[2554] They jumped up like two puppets on the same string. "You mean—"
[2555] "I mean," Rene said in a voice filled with sorrow, "the killer is dead."
[2556] A swan flapped its black-silk wings, splashing water on them.
[2557] "Please sit down, both of you," Rayne said, and they obeyed mechanically, "on the one hand, I'm glad you came today, on the other hand, not entirely. I still don't know if it's right to tell you this..."
[2558] Sam growled.
[2559] "No, Detective, I'm not a sadist enjoying watching you suffer," Rayne continued seriously, "this is a real problem."
[2560] "But why didn't you tell us, for God's sake?" Bruno shouted.
[2561] "Because," Reen said, "you wouldn't believe me."
[2562] A bead of sweat rolled down the inspector's nose and fell down his thick chin.
[2563] "It's just too unbelievable," Reen said calmly, "If, after hearing what I have to say, any of you kick me into the pool and accuse me of lying, being delusional, crazy, and as crazy as the Blackthorn family" – his voice trembled – "I wouldn't blame you."
[2564] "It's Louise Cabian," the prosecutor said slowly.
[2565] Reen gazed into his eyes: "No." He answered.

[2663] "Second contradiction. Do you remember the dust on the laboratory floor had many footprints, none of which were clear? These dust could not have any connection with Blackthorn's original plan. So those footprints and any conclusions drawn from them are included in the actual situation. We can clearly see that the user of the laboratory rubbed off all the clear footprints - on the one hand, it was very clever for a little boy, but there were no footprints, either worn or unworn, near the only door in the room! Okay, an adult would not ignore leaving footprints near the door, because he actually came in through the chimney, and this should be kept as a secret. Footprints near the door could mislead the police into thinking the intruder came in through the door, perhaps with a copied key. The absence of footprints near the door would definitely lead to an investigation of the fireplace. Again, as I said, a sign of an immature mind, ignoring the most obvious flaw in his actions - because he did think of rubbing off the footprints, and if it were an adult, he would not have missed this flaw either."
[2664] "Adding to that," Sam growled, "Oh, I'm such an idiot!"
[2665] "The third contradiction, I dare say, is the most intriguing of all." Raine's eyes sparkled momentarily. "You two—like me—are confused by the bizarre weapon used to kill Mrs. Blackthorn. York Blackthorn's method is clever: the oil is a 'smell' that points to the killer in the story, and it is because of this clue that he is caught at the end of the story. However, in real life, since Blackthorn is already dead, using a fragrance to arouse suspicion of York Blackthorn is not mature... Here we find what? A mind blindly following written instructions - an immature mind."
[2669] "The fourth contradiction, or is it the fifth? In Heath's story, he is the criminal himself and has planted a clue pointing to himself - the scent of vanilla. [2670] "However, when this ceased to be a story and became real life, the situation changed - someone took the plot of a novel as a model for real crime. So why, like the killer did, use two false clues pointing to two different people? Any adult in Jody's position would have chosen Conrad's shoes as a reliable false clue and discarded the vanilla-flavored one pointing to a dead person - at least, they would have chosen one or the other, not used both indiscriminately. If they had chosen the shoes, they would not have worn them like Jody did, but simply sprinkled poison on the tip of one shoe and left the shoe in Conrad's closet - that would have been enough. However, again, due to a lack of mature understanding of suggestions and explicit instructions, Jody actually wore the shoes - spilling talcum powder, which was not mentioned in the outline, was just an accident, proving that the outline did not require the shoes to be worn in order to leave footprints - and this was the only possible reason to wear the shoes... All this indicates that the killer, faced with a situation that required only the wisdom of a typical adult, failed to weigh the situation appropriately, once again, as I said, pointing to a childish act.
[2671] "Finally, the great fire. Before reading the outline, the fire confused me. Adults would probably just abandon it, as even in York's novels, it was a poor method and not a particularly clever detective story material.
[2672] "So, what are we dealing with? As for the reasons for doing these things, he does not understand; the only thing his mind grasps is: according to the blueprint, he knows York is the criminal, he knows York is dead, and he decides to be York, or the criminal himself.
